MEXICO
SECOND CONTRACTUAL SAVINGS DEVELOPMENT PROGRAM LOAN (CSDP II)
PROPOSED LOAN AND PROGRAM SUMMARY
BORROWER:         Banco Nacional de Obras y Servicios Puiblicos, SNC (BANOBRAS).
GUARANTOR:    United Mexican States.
AMOUNT:           US$400 million
TERMS:            The loan is proposed to be a fixed rate single currency loan in US dollars,
with a maturity of up to fifteen years.
OBJECTIVES:    The proposed loan would support the second phase of the Government's
Contractual Savings Development Program (CSDP), which is designed to:
(a) increase the equity, efficiency, and sustainability of the old age security
system and gradually lead to greater effective coverage; (b) implement a
financially viable pension system; (c) limit the fiscal impact of the current
pension system; (d) raise the level of institutional savings and improve the
allocative efficiency of domestic savings; and (e) enhance capital market
development by stimulating greater private financial intermediation.
DESCRIPTION:   The first phase of the reform focused on the establishment of the legal,
regulatory, and institutional framework for the reform of the country's old
age security system.  The second phase concentrates on the actual
implementation of the reformed pension system. Specifically, the second
phase of the CSDP supported by the proposed loan (CSDP II), included:
(a) implementing, consolidating, and fine-tuning the reformed system for
private sector workers by: (i) ensuring a flexible and responsive regulatory
framework; (ii) initiating operation of the reformed system and increasing
its efficiency; (iii) transferring SAR92 balances from the Central Bank to
individual AFORE accounts; (iv) fine-tuning investment policies and
improving risk management; (v) strengthening supervision and
enforcement; and (vi) initiating payment of disability and survivors benefits
by private insurers; (b) updating estimates of the fiscal costs of transition;
(c) deepening reforms to INFONAVIT to ensure adequate returns on the
housing sub-account; (d) advancing in the technical preparatory work for
the reform of the public pension systems; (e) sustaining public confidence
through education and information; and (f) advancing in the
implementation of complementary financial market initiatives.



- iv-
BENEFITS:          Overall, the program will lay the basis for a marked increase in the
volume of institutional savings and provide the institutional basis through
which domestic savings can be channeled to productive investment. It
will also lead to the creation of a new class of institutional investors and a
new set of longer-term instruments. Pension and social security reform
will move the actuarially bankrupt defined-benefit system to a fully
funded defined contribution scheme.   Public confidence will be
strengthened, as workers will no longer view their contributions as a tax
to provide benefits to current pensioners; instead, they will view these
contributions as a means to build personal wealth. The separation of the
pension and health sector schemes will result in pressure for greater
efficiency in the delivery of public health benefits. Reforrns in the
insurance industry will create a larger class of insurance products such as
annuities, life and disability and pre-paid medical insurance. Reforms to
improve Mexico's legal framework for mutual funds, secured lending and
asset backed securities will deepen the capital market over time and
broaden the base for domestic savings. In addition, over the longer term,
as pension funds increase their equity fund investments, they will become
major catalysts for improved corporate governance.
RISKS:             The reform could lead to higher fiscal costs if the savings generated from
defined wage contributions do not earn an adequate real rate of return.
These fiscal risks arise from the Government's continued guarantee of a
minimum pension benefit to everyone participating in the social security
system at the time of retirement. Without the housing fund component
earning a positive real rate of return, these risks are high, making
INFONAVIT reforms key to the scheme's sustainability. Given the legal
limitation on investment in foreign securities, the success of domestic
capital market reforms are also crucial to the success of the pension
reform especially because of the weakened state of the banking system,
which is currently the only major private source of financial assets. Low
real rates of return on the average pension fund's assets are also likely to
increase the moral hazard associated with providing a minimum pension
guarantee by encouraging contributor preference for high risk pension
investments.
The risks of political pressures to reverse fiscal management policies will
always exist but are mitigated by Mexico's growth potential over the
medium term. On the banking side, specific programs are in place to
increase the capitalization of the banking system while the overall level
of supervision  has improved  markedly.   CNBV's strengthened
supervisory role for INFONAVIT should ensure the viability of its
operational and financial restructuring program. All the above factors
will be important for improving the likelihood that pension funds will
earn reasonable real rates of return, thus reducing moral hazard concerns.



POVERTY
CATEGORY:           Not Applicable.
ESTIMATED
DISBURSEMENT:  The proposed loan will be disbursed in a single tranche of US$400
million upon loan effectiveness (October 1998).



REPORT AND RECOMMENDATION OF THE PRESIDENT
OF THE INTERNATIONAL BANK FOR RECONSTRUCTION AND DEVELOPMENT
TO THE EXECUTIVE DIRECTORS
ON A PROPOSED
SECOND C'ONTRACTUAL SAVINGS DEVELOPMENT PROGRAM LOAN
TO BANOBRAS WITH THE GUARANTEE OF THE UNITED MEXICAN STATES
1.1   I submit for your approval the following Report and Recommendation on a proposed
Loan to the Banco Nacional de Obras y Servicios Pzublicos, SNC (BANOBRAS) with the
guarantee of the United Mexican States, in the amount of US$400 million to support the second
phase of the Government's Contractual Savings Development Program (CSDP II) which focuses
on the implementation of the legal, regulatory, and institutional framework for the reform of the
country's old age security system. The loan would be single currency loan at the Bank's
standard fixed rate, with a maturity of up to 15 years, and would be disbursed in a single trariche
of US$400 million.
I. MACROECONOMIC CONTEXT AND FINANCING REQUIREMENTS
1.2   Recent Economic Developments and Prospects. The Mexican economy rebounded in
1996-97, following the financial crisis of end-1994 and the severe recession of 1995 when GDP
fell by 6.2 percent and domestic investment dropped by an excess of 30 percent. In 1996, real
GDP rose by 5.2 percent reflecting continued export expansion and a strong recovery in
investment. The implementation of tight financial policies led to a near halving of inflation, a
significant lowering of nominal interest rates, and a greater stability of the peso. Investor
sentiment improved, leading to a substantial rise in foreign direct investment, a marked
improvement in Mexico's access to international capital markets, and a substantial increase in
international reserves. Preliminary data suggest a consolidation of the economic recovery in
1997, while inflation continues to decline. Real GDP growth reached 7 percent in the year with
another healthy expansion of investment in excess of 20 percent.
1.3   As other ermerging market economies, Mexico was jolted by the events in Southeast Asia
in the second half of 1997. But, in Mexico, the contagion effects from Southeast Asia were so
far relatively small when compared to events in other emerging market economies. Areas of
vulnerability of the Mexican economy to adverse external developments are likely to be found in
(i) the international oil price,  (ii) capital flows to emerging markets and (iii) trade
competitiveness.
1.4   Mexico's external accounts experienced a marked improvement since 1994. Exports
boomed in 1995 prompted by the large change in the exchange rate and by the contraction in
domestic demand; and these same forces led to an opposite impact on imports. With the
expansion of domestic output and demand as of 1996, imports recovered rapidly whereas export
growth was reduced to more sustainable levels. The trade balance observed a surplus in 1995
and 1996, of 7.1 and 6.5 billion dollar respectively, which diminished rapidly to 0.6 billion dollar
in 1997 and is likely to become negative over the next few years.



2                                                                     PREsIDENT7SREPORT
1.5   A steady appreciation of the real exchange rate has been observed over the past two and
half years and can be attributed mainly to a resumption of capital flows. The combination of an
appreciating real exchange rate and growing domestic absorption contributed to a reversal in the
trade account and an increasing deficit on the current account. In 1997, the deficit in the current
account exceeded US$ 7.3 billion, and it is expected that it will continue to expand in 1998.
Nevertheless, on balance, the level of the deficit (at about 2-3 percent of GDP), and of external
borrowing, is prudent and consistent with a solid long-term creditworthiness position.
1.6   Underlying the strong performance of the external accounts and of private sector has been
the supportive role of government. The operations of the public sector resulted in an increase in
the primary surplus from 2.2 percent of GDP in 1994 to 4.5 percent of GDP in 1995-1996, in a
context of overall fiscal balance which was prolonged in 1997. Despite declines of government
expenditures reflected in transfers to public enterprises, lower spending on wages and salaries,
and a rephasing of some capital projects, expenditure on social programs was safeguarded.
Monetary policy has supported the fiscal stance and has focused, primarily, on disinflation.
Monthly rates of inflation have been on a declining trend since end-1995 and, as a result, the
annual rate of inflation has fallen from 52 percent in 1995 to 28 percent in 1996 and to less than
16 percent in 1997.
1.7   The short- and medium-term prognosis for the conduct of macro policy, hence for the
economy as a whole, is positive. In 1998, real growth is expected to slow down to a more
sustainable rate of about 4.8 percent with continued expansion in investment and progress in
disinflation. However, the public sector balance is expected to deteriorate for structural and
policy-induced reasons, even as the fiscal authorities come under increasing political pressure for
authorizing expenditure increases and/or revenue reductions.
1.8   Justification for Adjustment Lending. The recent decline of international oil prices has
led to additional pressures on public sector revenues, with oil income responsible for an average
of 38 percent of total public sector revenue. The lower levels of oil prices observed in particular
as of December 1997, prompted the Government to announce expenditure reductions of an
amount of 0.7 percent of GDP for 1998 in order to maintain its public deficit target at 1.25% of
GDP. While the estimated public revenue reduction to date has been mainly on account of
external sales and those domestic sales that are contractually tied to price developments on world
markets (e.g. natural gas and fuel-oil), a longer lasting weakness of international oil prices will
increase pressures to reflect part of the international price decline in other oil related products
sold on the domestic market, such as gasoline and diesel.
1.9   The contractual savings reform program as implemented by the Mexican government as
of 1997, adequately addresses one of the major areas of public contingent liabilities. The reform,
covering workers in the private sector, is causing a near term pressure on public finances with an
estimated annual fiscal cost of 1.5 percent of GDP. Furthermore, other quasi-fiscal losses and
contingent liabilities were allowed to expand and will require additional fiscal outlays in the near
future. These losses arise mainly from the successive bailouts and/or rescheduling of the debts
held by private banks, firms, households (mortgages and credit card debt), tollways operators,
etc., in the wake of the 1995 financial crisis. The total cost of the financial sector rescue



MEXco - SECOND CONTRACTUAL SAVINGS DEVELOPMENTPROGRAM(CSDP II)                  3
programs are now estimated at 14 percent of GDP, or a near term annual pressure on public
finances of at least another 0.6 percent of GDP.
1.10   Admittedly, at the level of 40 percent of GDP, Mexico now holds one of the lowest
levels of public debt among OECD countries. Yet it also faces the unique challenge of providing
an adequate social and productive infrastructure for its expanding and aspiring population, and
for its increasingly competitive firms. Public investment now stands at about 3 percent of GDP
per year, and this may be insufficient to meet future challenges. Moreover, an overhaul of the
fiscal-federalist system is overdue, with a significant shift in the relative balance of fiscal
capabilities between the federation and the states. The combination of greater pressure on
expenditure with an unavoidable recognition of contingent losses could threaten the medium
term fiscal prograin.
1.11  In these conditions, maintaining fiscal discipline is essential as is the policy of sustaining
a careful balance between domestic and foreign sources of funding of the fiscal deficit. Given
the success of its recent macroeconomic performance, and the credibility of its policies, Mexico
again enjoys an arnple access to international financial markets and, thus, did not face a funding
constraint for its balance of payments requirements. Nevertheless, the current account deficit for
1998 is expected to be approximately 3% of GDP, and could in fact be higher. Hence,
maintaining adequate access to medium-term financing as provided by IBRD is an essential
element of the Government's strategy. Moreover, the Government faces a seriously binding
fiscal constraint. rhe current program of reforms would exacerbate the difficulties to be faced by
the government in abiding to this constraint. World Bank support would allow the authorities
more room to maneuver. It would safeguard the limits of domestic borrowing and improve the
overall quality of the medium-term fiscal response.
II.  THE GOVERNMENT'S CONTRACTUAL SAVINGS REFORM PROGRAM
2.1   This section presents the genesis and overall objectives of the Government's contractual
savings reform  and its medium-term  program.  This section also highlights the main
characteristics of the World Bank's support through a phased reform approach. Finally, it
summarizes accornplishments in the initial stage of contractual savings reform.
A.   Genesis and Objectives of the Pension Reform
2.2   Since coming into power, the Zedillo administration recognized that Mexico's contractual
savings system  suffered from  several weaknesses.   These have significant economic
consequences that limit the viability of the social insurance system, constrain the development of
the country's financial sector, and limit prospects for sustainable economic growth. The
Government of Mexico has embarked on a major and important reform of the old age security
system which should prove beneficial both to individuals, through the opportunity to obtain
increased retirement income, and to Mexico's economic growth prospects over the medium and
long term.
2.3   Demographic changes, rising health costs, as well as an increased level of benefits not
properly backed in sufficient actuarial reserves indicated that the public pay-as-you-go (PAYG)
pension system operating in Mexico, would become financially unsustainable by the end of the



4                                                                   PRESIDENT'SREPORT
decade. A limited and failed attempt at reform was made in 1992 by establishing a mandatory
individual retirement savings account (Sistema de Ahorro para el Retiro - SAR92) - with
mandatory 2% defined contribution individual accounts - and by raising the contribution quota to
the Mexican Social Security Institute (Instituto Mexicano de Seguridad Social - IMSS). The
worsening financial situation of IMSS, labor market distortions, unfairness linked to the benefits'
scheme, and the need to generate higher domestic savings - particularly after the December 1994
crisis - led to a growing consensus for pension reformn.
2.4   The reform of contractual savings is one of the key goals in the Government's National
Development Plan (1995-2000). It is a medium-term program aimed at:
* increasing the equity, efficiency, and sustainability of the old age security system
gradually leading to greater effective coverage;
* implementing a financially viable pension system;
1 limiting the fiscal impact of the current pension system and ensure transparency of the
fiscal costs of transition;
* enhancing financial market development and reducing volatility by stimulating
greater private financial intermediation and increasing the array of financial
instruments and contracts available; and
l  contributing to enhance the allocative efficiency of domestic and, in the longer-term,
to raising aggregate savings.
B.   The Government's Medium-Term Program
2.5   The initial stage of reform. The shift of a country's pension system to a more equitable
and financially sustainable one and the implementation of complementary financial sector reforms,
is a long-term process. The Mexican Government could not afford - financially and politically - to
tackle all aspects of the reform in one fell swoop. Also, the fine-tuning of the reform depends on
the outcome of the implementation of the initial phase. The Government decided to initially focus
its efforts towards the reform of the old age insurance scheme for private sector workers. During
1995-96 the Government implemented a number of policy actions that resulted in shift from a
public PAYG, defined-benefit system to a privately managed, defined-contribution system. Also,
the Government set up the supervisory framework for the private pension fund administrators and
issued the investment rules to be followed by the pension funds. Section C below summarizes the
key accomplishments of this initial stage of reform implemented during 1995-1996, and supported
by the World Bank's fist Contractual savings development Program Loan (CSDP I).
2.6   The remaining agenda. Full realization of the objectives of the reform depend on the
consolidation and fine-tuning of the reforms initiated during 1995-96, on maintaining the
necessary flexibility to cope with implementation challenges, as well as addressing a number of
design limitations. The remaining agenda needs to address the following strategic issues:
c Increase the efficiency of the reformed system to lower administrative costs and
augment the amount of funds available for investment. This challenge entails:
the successful operation of the AFORE industry, including authorization, affiliation,
and switching;



MEaCO - SECOND CONTRACTUAL SA vINGSDEVELOPMENTPROGRAM (CSDP 11)                  5
* the smooth and efficient operation of the new centralized system for collection,
individualization of accounts, and record-keeping;
* The development of pension performance benchmarks for investment return and
pension plan service.
* Ensure that CONSAR continues delivering:
* A flexible regulatory environment - able to respond to the changing needs of the
reformed system. Key short-term actions include the issuance of rules relating to: (i)
switching among AFOREs (traspasos); (ii) disclosure; (iii) transfer of SAR 92
balances into individual accounts; and (iv) orderly consolidation of the industry; and
* A strong supervisory framework with enhanced enforcement capacity.
1 Create the conditions for adequate risk-adjusted returns for AFOREs. Critical to
contain fiscal costs for transition generation and to ensure adequate retirement income to new
generations. The focus should be on:
* fine-tuning the investment regime and expanding investment opportunities; and
* Strengthening risk management systems.
D Design and implement mechanisms to ensure a competitive return to the contributions
channeled to the housing sub-account (INFONAVIT). The reform will not fully succeed
unless adequate returns can be assured on the 5% contribution to INFONAVIT - which
represents 37% of total contribution for a worker earning the average wage.
> Improve the efficiency of the provision of life and disability insurance and foster
continued delvelopment of the insurance industry and annuities markets. Disability and
life continues to be provided by IMSS with a fixed charge of 2.5% of payroll. The cost is
high compared to that observed in other Latin American countries that have opted for
allowing competition by pension fund mangers and the purchase of the insurance from an
insurance company.
*  Continue monitoring of fiscal cost estimates, including sensitivity analysis. The Mexican
plan is riskier on the fiscal side than the approach followed in other Latin American countries
because the Government's liability is uncertain. The reformed system allows for a lifetime
switch between the old and new system for every transition worker. Mexican fiscal costs, as
compared to Chile's, for example, could be (i) higher, if returns on individual contributions
(AFORES + INFONAVIT) are not high enough to ensure that a large number of transition
workers choose, at retirement, the new system, but could be (ii) lower, because Mexico is
saving the cost of recognition bonds if the market value at retirement of individual
accumulated balances exceeds accrued benefits under the old PAYG system. Also, the
Government did not restructure the current PAYG system as part of the 1997 reform and it
continues to be a nominal system with many structural weaknesses that make the results of
the reform program very sensitive to the future rate of inflation.
* Sustain the public confidence in the system by building public support and confidence in
the new pension system so as to avoid evasion and attract informal workers.
* Accelerate complementary financial sector reforms to ensure an adequate supply of
quality financial assets available for pension fund investment and support the development of
financial infrastructure needs (such as custody services and rating agencies).



6                                                                 PRESIDENT'SPREPORT
* Expand the reform to Public Sector workers. The current reform did not contemplate
changes to the ISSSTE, PEMEX, other parastatals, armed forces, and the state pension plans.
Hence, the portability constraint across private and public sector workers' pension plans will
remain for now an issue along with the financial disequilibrium to be faced in the future by
these pension funds.
* Expand coverage. One of the longer-term objectives of the Mexican reform is to extend
coverage and minimize the incidence of old-age poverty. Even in Chile, where the AFP
system has been in full operation for nearly 16 years, that portion of the economically active
population that is informally employed has increased from 49.9% in 1990 to 51.2% in 1995.
This suggests that the removal of labor-market distortions via pension reform, may be a
necessary but not sufficient condition either for formalization of the factors of production, or
for widespread participation in the privatized pension system.
* Allow investment in equities and in foreign securities. The explicit legal prohibition on
investment in foreign securities was introduced by Congress as an amendment to the draft
legislation proposed by the Executive branch. This prohibition is based on the argument that
it would not be desirable to channel abroad long-term savings required by the domestic
economy. However, this prohibition restricts the possibility of diversifying the investment
portfolio and inhibits diversification of country risk.
C.   World Bank Support: A Phased Reform Approach
2.7   The World Bank's support in the area of contractual savings in Mexico began in 1990
with the preparation of an economic report, which provided a detailed analysis of contractual
savings issues. The proposed loan is the outcome of a close dialogue between the Bank and the
Government on the development of Mexico's financial sector and the need to improve the
allocation of financial resources and set the basis for the effective channeling of domestic
savings. The Bank's participation accelerated in late-1994 with the provision of advice and
technical support in the area of pension reform and capital market development. This culminated
with the decision to support the Mexican Government's reform objectives through a series of
one-tranche adjustment operations.
2.8   To provide a strategic framework, this section highlights: (i) policy actions supported
under the first Contractual Savings Development Program Loan (CSDP I) - approved in
December 13, 1996; (ii) policy actions supported by the proposed CSDP II; and (iii) the key
benchmarks of further progress in addressing remaining issues (para. 2.6) which could be
supported in follow-up operation (s). The Policy Matrix in Annex II provides additional details
on the medium-term framework.
¢ 1995-96policyreformssupported by CSDPI (paras.2.9-2.19):
/ established the legal and regulatory framework to shift the pension system for private
sector workers to a defined contribution, privately-managed scheme;
/ issued the investment management rules for the pension funds;
v strengthened the supervisory agency (CONSAR);
/ estimated fiscal transition costs, including sensitivity analysis;
,/ took first steps to improve the financial performance of INFONAVIT;

V initiatecl public awareness campaign to educate the public and bolster support for the
reform; and
V initiatecl the implementation of complementary financial market initiatives.
*  January 1997-April 1998 policy reforms supported by the proposed CSDP II (para. 3.1):
V  implemented, consolidated, and fine-tuned the reform to the pension system for private
sector workers;
/  successiully initiated operation of centralized collection and record-keeping system;
V advanced in the reform of INFONAVIT to ensure adequate returns on the housing sub-
account;
/ updated estimates and sensitivity analysis of the fiscal costs of the reform;
/  advanced in the technical preparatory work for the public sector and state pension
systems;

V  continued implementation of complementary financial market reforms.
. Future Agenda for World Bank support - benchmarks for a third phase:
*  demonstrate clear results in complying with INFONAVIT reform targets as specified
in the Convenio;
D  strengthen CONSAR's independence;
*  introduce greater portfolio diversification including investment in equities;
D  design and implement measures to contain the administrative costs of the system;
D implement measures to reduce the cost of the disability and life insurance scheme;
. ensure the financial viability of the pension schemes for public sector workers; and
. identify specific actions to increase coverage if reformed system has no significant
impact.
D.   Key Accomplishments under the First Phase of the Contractual Savings Reform
2.9    The reform covers private sector workers and self-employed - approximately 12 million
contributors. These workers represent approximately 80% of the labor force in the formal sector. It
is estimated that approximately US$4 billion will flow into the system annually (Figure 1).
2.10    Along the lines of recent pension reform programs in Latin America, the Mexican design
is based on a multipillar approach to old age security. The reformed scheme consists of:
* a publicly managed first pillar with a redistributive objective providing a guaranteed
minimum pension equal to the indexed minimum wage for low-income workers
meetingJ the legal requirements;
*  a fully-funded second pillar with mandatory individual savings accounts and
competitive, but exclusive and specialized, mutual fund management, and
* a third pillar consisting of voluntary savings.
2.11  Legal Framework. In December 1995 the Mexican Congress approved legislation (the
new Social security law - Ley del Seguro Social) modifying the public PAYG, defined benefits



8                                                                   PRESIDENT'SREPORT
scheme to establish a privately managed, mandatory, defined contributions scheme permitting
private management for private sector workers affiliated to IMSS. A second legislative package on
the implementation of the reforms (the Pension Systems Law - Ley de los Sistemas de Ahorro para
el Retiro) was enacted in April 1996. Legislation was also approved in December 1996 to make the
legislative framework for the housing scheme (INFONAVIT) consistent with the new pension
system. The reform became effective on July 1, 1997.
i ~~~12
2  6
2.12 Institutional responsibilities. Individual retirement funds (SIEFORES -Sociedades de
Inversion Especializadas en Fondos para el Retiro) are administered by investment management
firms (AFORES - Administradores de Fondos de Ahorro para el Retiro) established by the
private sector and IMSS. INFONAVIT retains the 5% housing sub-account and will provide
returns based on its operating surplus. IMSS, in addition to managing one of the AFOREs,
continues to be the legal enforcer of all contributions and collections, the provider of benefits to
transition workers choosing the old PAYG system; and the administrator of disability and life
insurance - although benefits are provided by private insurers.
2.13 Eligibility, contributions, and benefits. The reform separated the old age, severance,
and disability and life system (JVCM - Jnvalidez, Vejez, Cesantia, y Muerte) into the Old Age
and Severance insurance (RCV - Seguro de Retiro, Cesantia en Edad Avanzada, y Ve]ez) and the
Disability and Life Insurance (IV - Seguro de Invalidez y Vida). Each worker's individual
retirement account consists of two compulsory sub-accounts, the RCV sub-account managed by
the AFORES, and the INFONAVIT sub-account. Under the reform, contributions to old age and



MExCO- SECOND CONTRACTUAlLSA VINGSDEVELOPMENTPROGRAM (CSDP 11)                   9
severance in the AFORES' individual accounts equal 6.5% of a worker's wage and a
Government contribution known as the social quota. The social quota equals between 1 and
5.5% of the worker's wage depending on the level of each workers' wage and will be indexed to
the July 1, 1997 CPI. On average it will be equivalent to 2.2% of wages. It will be transferred to
each worker's account to which active contributions are being made. Transition workers have
the right to transfer their past SAR92 balances into the chosen AFORE. Contributions to the
INFONAVIT sub-account, equal to 5% of a worker's salary, will continue to be invested in
INFONAVIT housing. Total contributions for retirement, therefore, amount to between 12.0 and
17 percent of a worker's wage - for the worker earning the average wage (approximately 2.8
times the minimurn wages) contributions represent approximately 13.5% of the wage.
2.14 For workers who do not choose an AFORE, contributions go to a Central Account
(Cuenta Concentradora) kept at the Central Bank (Banco de Mexico) for a maximum period of
four years. A 2%Xo real return on accumulated balances is guaranteed. At the end of the four
years, CONSAR will assign an AFORE to each affiliate.
2.15  Benefits uader the new system fall into two groups: benefits to new workers and benefits
to transition workers. For new workers - defined as those beginning their contributions to the
system after July 1, 1997 - benefits will consist of the accumulated balances in their individual
retirement accounts (AFORES plus INFONAVIT accounts) or the Minimum Pension Guarantee
(MPG) to equal to the minimum wage in Mexico City as of July 1, 1997, indexed to the
Consumer Price Index (CPI). At retirement, workers will choose between a gradual withdrawal
option or buying en annuity from an insurance company.
2.16  Transition workers are defined as those workers contributing to the PAYG system prior
to July 1, 1997. Although all workers began contributing to their new individual retirement
accounts, transition workers retain the right to the benefits of the old system exclusively in the
case of retirement (lifetime switch option). The lifetime switch option allows transition
generation workers to choose at retirement the higher of the acquired benefits under the old
PAYG system and the accumulatfu balances in their individual retirement accounts under the
new system (AFCIRES plus INFONAVIT accounts). Accumulated balances in the INFONAVIT
sub-account (accumulated between 1992 and August 1997) and the SAR92 balances
(accumulated between 1992 and August 1997) will be surrendered to transition workers upon
retirement regard:less of which pension scheme they opt for. If the old system is chosen, the
MPG is equal to one Mexico City minimum wage indexed to the actual minimum wage.
2.17 The Government provides the MPG to both transition and new workers in the event that
the value of the worker's accumulated savings in all retirement accounts is inadequate to provide
for the minimum pension. The Government contribution will only be equal to the difference
between the MPG and funds available in the individual retirement accounts. The years of
contributions required for eligibility for the MPG were raised from 10 to 25 years (1250 weeks).
2.18  Supervision. The Pension System Law gives CONSAR (Comisi6n Nacional del Sistema
de Ahorro para el Retiro) broad powers to set and enforce rules and standards for all aspects of
operation of the reformed pension system. CONSAR has full supervisory authority over
AFORES and SIEFORES, as well as supervisory authority over other participants in the pension



10                                                                              PRESIDENT' SREPORT
system (such as banks and insurance companies) to the extent the activities of such entities
involve the pension system.
2.19 Administration. The key characteristics of the AFORE industry are as follows:
*  Nature and control of AFOREs. AFORES are single-purpose business corporations with
independent capitalization and minimum capital and special investment reserve requirements.
The paid in capital and special reserve are required to be invested in shares of the SIEFORES
managed by the AFORE.
*  Foreign ownership. Regulations allow for a class of AFORES majority-controlled by foreign
financial institutions (but not individuals or industrial concerns) to up to 49% of the capital of
the AFORE. Financial institutions from NAFTA countries, Colombia, Costa Rica and
Venezuela may establish majority-owned AFORES.
*  Conflicts of interest. The Pension Systems Law establishes strict limitations on permissible
transactions between AFORES and affiliated financial institutions and issuers.
*  Initially Single Class of SIEFORES.  The Pension Systems Law authorizes CONSAR to
permit each AFORE to manage and offer to its customers a variety of SIEFORES with
different portfolio compositions, provided that each AFORE offers at least one SIEFORE
whose portfolio is composed "fundamentally" of securities whose returns are indexed to the
Mexican CPI. There are no minimum return requirements for the AFORES. At the outset
CONSAR will allow each AFORE to establish only a single SIEFORE, "fundamentally"
invested in indexed instruments. CONSAR intends eventually to permit each AFORE to
offer customers a variety of equity and debt funds with distinct investment policies. This
decision to restrict each AFORE to only a single debt fund at the outset reflects the
government's desire to simplify supervision in the first year of pension fund operations,
reduce potential confusion among the public and build public support by avoiding volatility.

MEXICO - SECOND CONTRACTUAL SA VINGSDEVELOPMENTPROGRAM (CSDP II) 11
IH. THE SECOND PHASE OF THE CONTRACTUAL SAVINGS PROGRAM
3.1   The policy actions supported by the proposed second Contractual Savings Development
Program Loan (CSDP II) include:
(A) implementing, consolidating, and fine-tuning the reformed pension system for
private sector workers by:
(1) Ensuring a flexible and responsive regulatory framework:
(2) Initiating operation of the reformed system and increasing its efficiency:
(a) AFORE industry authorization, affiliation, and switching;
(b) smooth and efficient operation of new centralized system for
collection, individualization of accounts, and record keeping;
(c) Identifying opportunities for cost cutting and development of pension
performance benchmarks;
(3) Transferring SAR92 balances from the Central Bank to individual AFORE
accounts;
(4) Fine-tuning investment policies and improving risk management;
(5) Strengthening supervision and enforcement; and
(6) Initiating provision of disability and survivors benefits by private insurers.
(B) updating estimates of the fiscal costs of transition, including sensitivity analysis;
(C) deepeining reforms to INFONAVIT to ensure adequate returns on the housing sub-
account;
(D) advancing in the technical preparatory work for the public (ISSSTE) and State
pension systems by finalizing in the actuarial and financial diagnosis and evaluating
alternative reforn strategies;
(E) sustaining public confidence through education and information; and
(F) advancing in the implementation of complementary financial market initiatives
in: (1) the banking sector; (2) mutual funds; (3) occupational and voluntary pension
plans; and (4) the development of new instruments in the long-term Government debt
market, the corporate debt market, and the housing mortgage market.
The main issues in these policy areas and the Government's response are described below.
A. Implementation and Consolidation of the 1997 Pension Reform
Al. Regulatory Framework
3.2   Timely and flexible regulation of pension funds by CONSAR will play a major role in
ensuring the success of the reform. Though justifiable as initial conditions or explained by the
fragility of the financial sector and political constraints, some features of the initial regulatory
framework may limit the most productive long term investments and/or require more flexibility
as the system evolves. Short-term actions include the issuance by CONSAR of rules relating to:
(i) disclosure; (ii) administration of individual accounts in Central Account (Cuenta
Concentradora); (iii) limits in market share; (iv) switching among AFOREs (traspasos); (v)



12                                                                 PRESIDENT' sREPORT
collection; (vi) commissions; (vii) transfer of SAR 92 balances; (viii) investment management;
and (ix) sanctions.
3.3   To ensure the flexibility of the regulatory framework and to respond to additional
regulatory needs after the initial implementation of the reform, CONSAR has issued a number of
key regulations (Circulares) since January 1997 (see Technical Annex Table A13 for a listing of
key regulations issued by CONSAR as of March 13, 1998). This section will focus on
regulations relating to disclosure and administration of individual accounts still in the Central
Account. Rules and guidelines relating to the other areas are discussed later in the relevant
sections.
3.4   Disclosure. In the reformed system there are three main flows of information: from the
participants in the operation of the system (AFOREs, SIEFOREs, PROCESAR) to CONSAR;
from the AFOREs to the affiliates; and from CONSAR to the affiliates and to Congress (See
Table Al 3 in technical Annex for a list of reports required by CONSAR). The key regulations
issued by CONSAR relating to disclosure [Board Presentation la] include guidelines for:
o general information disclosure from entities involved in the operation of the system to
CONSAR (Circular 19-1 [11/17/97]);
* accounting and balance sheet information to be sent by AFOREs and SIEFOREs to
CONSAR (Circulars 12-1 [4/16/97] and 12-4 [3/11/98]);
* the monthly report to be sent by each AFORE to CONSAR (Circular 25-1 [12/11/97]);
* general information disclosure by AFOREs to affiliates and the public in general (Circular 9-
1 [1/10/97]);
 prospectus for each SIEFORE (Circulars 10-1 [1/14/97] and 10-2 [6/30/97]);
* Individual Statement to be sent to workers' with the financial performance and balances of
the AFORE and INFONAVIT accounts (Circular 8-2 [2/12/98]). A sample of the required
format is contained in Table Al4a of the Technical Annex; and
o Confidentiality of individual account information by entities involved in the operation of the
system (Circular 23-1 [12/2/97]).
3.5   "The AFORE maquiladora": administration of individual accounts still in the
Central Account. As of the end of February 1998, 2.3 million workers had not yet chosen an
AFORE and their accumulated balances are currently in the Cuenta Concentradora held at the
Central Bank (para 2.12). CONSAR has issued rules relating to the outsourcing of informnation
to workers in the Cuenta Concentradora (Circular 27-1 [2/20/98]) [Board presentation lb(i)].
This service is to be provided by.the AFOREs - under a scheme to be called AFORE
Maquiladora. The regulation also determines the principles upon which interested AFOREs will
be assigned these accounts (initially, it is based on the number of CURPs generated by the
AFORE as of September 30,1997). The AFORE maquiladora will charge a fee of 1. 15% of flows
(according to general guidelines issued by CONSAR in Circular 29-1 on February 27, 1998)
[Board Presentation lb(ii)]. These workers' individual accounts at the Central Bank will
continue to pay a return of 2% above inflation - compared to the 6% real return currently posted
on average by the AFOREs. It is expected that this system will foster additional affiliation to the



MExCO - SECOND CONTRACTUAL SA VINGSDEVELOPMENTPROGRAm (CSDP 11)                                  13
AFORE system, since it will allow the AFOREs to access the database of workers still remaining
in the Cuenta Concentradora.

A2. Operation and Efficiency of the New Pension System
(a) AFORE industry authorization, affiliation, market share limits, and switching rules
3.6    Authorization process.  CONSAR  has authorized 17 AFOREs in a timely fashion,
ensuring that foreign-owned AFOREs receive equal treatment with Mexican-owned AFOREs
[Board presentation 2a] (Table 1 above).
3.7    Affiliation Process. CONSAR issued the general rules governing the affiliation contract
between the worker and his/her chosen AFORE on February 18, 1997 (Circular 11-1) [Board
Presentation 2bl. Since then, there has been an extremely positive initial response to the new
system - as demonstrated by the affiliation of over 98% of potential workers by end-February
1998 (Figure 2a) '. In early 1997, the expectation was for approximately 60% of potential IMSS
affiliates to choose an AFORE during the first year of operation of the system [Board
'Note that the size of the market was subsequently increased (para. 3.9).

Presentation 2c]. As of February 28, 1998 assets under management by the AFOREs totaled
P$13.7 billion - equivalent to approximately US$1.6 billion (Figure 2b).

3.8    Market share limits and concentration of the industry. The law stated that for four
years - beginning in 1997 - there would be a 17% maximum market share limit for each AFORE.
This limit could then be increase to 20% after 4 years. CONSAR issued regulations which
determine that the limit is determined in terms of number of workers affiliated to IMSS registered
in each AFORE. To assure that no AFORE receives an unfair competitive advantage, CONSAR

authorizations to a significant number of competitors (Figures 3a and 3b).

3.9 The originval estimate of the potential IMSS affiliates for 1998 - based upon which the
initial 17% limit on the market share of AFOREs was calculated - was 11.7 million workers.
During the initial months of implementation, it has became clear that this number did not include
"inactive" workers, i.e., those workers who affiliated to an AFORE but are not currently
contributing to the social security system. Inactive workers are currently estimated at

approximately 3.2 million workers. As a result, CONSAR will issue a circular specifying that

3.10    Switching (Traspasos). CONSAR issued rules 24-1 (December 10, 1997) and 28-1
(January 8, 1998) regulating the switching of workers' accounts among AFOREs [Board
Presentation 2d]. A heated debate preceded the publication of these rules. As could be expected,
the smaller AFOREs felt that affiliates should be presented with as few barriers as possible in
order to be able to switch funds (e.g., avoid excessive bureaucracy). The larger AFOREs, on the
contrary, would have liked to see stronger barriers to switching. The experience of Chile and
Argentina - where almost 30% of total cost accounts for vendors (promotores) trying to
encourage switching among fund administrators - highlights the importance of regulating and
limiting the cost of this process. In this context, there is a consensus that the one year limitation
for switching - expect in cases where the commission structure is modified - is an improvement
over other reforms.  Tables A17a and A17b in the Technical Annex describe in detail the
switching scheme - through an intermediary or directly between AFOREs, respectively.



MExWCo - SECOND CONTRACTUAL SA ViNGSDEVELOPMENTPROGRAM (CSDP 11)                 17
(b) Smooth anad efficient operation of new centralized system for collection,
individualizationL of accounts, and record keeping
3.11  Mexico decided to follow a centralized scheme for contributions collection and
information gathering. Despite its theoretical advantages, the Argentinean experience has shown
that this kind of scheme might lead to operational and incentive problems. The Mexican version
of this process faced a critical problem in the worker's ID number - which was one of the key
factors behind the failure of the SAR 1992 reform. There are other risks present in the
Argentinean scheme that may appear in Mexico, increasing operation costs and reducing the
workers' balances: delays in the flow of money and information due to excessive operations or
identification problems in the national data base.
3.12  The enactment by the Assembly on October 28, 1996 of legislation postponing the
implementation of the social security reform from January 1 to July 1, 1997 sought to address
this concern. At that time, the Government felt it was not ready with the unique identification
system, which is essential to avoid evasion, ghosting etc, and had not yet addressed all issues
relating to systems for collection of contributions and their transfer to the AFORES. Also, at that
time, the Mexican authorities, decided that it was necessary to install a more powerful system for
individualization of accounts and to move towards a centralized and more reliable and
accountable computer-oriented scheme for the payment of contributions.
3.13  The objectives of the integrated collection system are to guarantee the quality of the
information in the individualized accounts; integrate collection of all social security payments
(including INFONAVIT contributions) in a unique contribution payment system; facilitate
payment by employers; and facilitate conciliation and control by social security institutions. In
fact, in a fully funded defined contribution system, information is as critical as the actual funds -
a payment without the correct information will not be deposited in the workers' account. The
specific operational requirements were: (i) a unique identification number for each worker; (ii) a
unique system for compliance with employers' obligations; (iii) the existence of appropriate and
homogeneous infi^astructure in the institutions receiving the payments; (iv) a centralized system
for the identification of individual accounts; (v) an efficient system for the reconciliation of
payments and data; (vi) computerized entry system for employers to avoid inconsistent
information; (vii) coordination among participants to the system; and (viii) a telecommunications
network to integrate all participants to the process.
3.14  Individualization and record-keeping responsibilities have been given as a concession to a
privately managed data collection firm (PROCESAR) that receives daily information on fund
flows and affiliates. PROCESAR was given authorization to manage the system's data base,
including the following obligations: (i) receive the information from the authorized banks on
payments received; (ii) reconcile this information with the funds deposited; and (iii)
individualize the lunds and ensure that they are deposited in the appropriate AFORE.
3.15  The basic tax collection activity for pension funds in Mexico requires that each covered
private sector employer determines what each employee owes, and then deducts this amount
from each employee's gross salary. At month's end, and within a pre-determinated number of
working days, the employer must deposit the amount owed at special authorized banks



18                                                                PRESIDENT'SREPORT
(Entidades receptoras). Individual AFORES and their agents do not have an active role to play
in the week-in and week-out grass-roots tax collection effort in Mexico (unlike in Chile). Table
A10 in the Technical Annex contains a summary chart of the information and money flows and
the institutions involved in the process.
3.16  To ensure the smooth and efficient operation of the collection and record-keeping
process, the following actions have been taken by the authorities:
.  Regulatory framework. CONSAR has issued the following Circulares regulating the
functioning of the collection and record-keeping system: 7-1 (1/9/97); 16-1(6/30/97); 20-1
(11/11/97); 22-1 (12/10/97); 20-2 (3/2/98) [Board Presentation 2e].
*  Unique identification number. In terms of account individualization, it was decided to
initially utilize the IMSS and gradually move to a more reliable identification number called
Clave Unica de Registro de Poblacion (CURP). The CURP is expected to eliminate
duplication of accounts and diminish transaction costs. As of February 28, 1998 over 4
million CURPs had been generated - equivalent to approximately 34% of potential accounts
(Table A12 - Technical Annex) [Board Presentation 211.
*  Computerized collection system. A Unique Autodetermination System (SUA - Sistema
Unico de Autodeterminacion) was developed and agreed upon by all relevant parties on May
27, 1997 [Board Presentation 2g]. The SUA consists of a computer (diskette-based)
software program for the reporting of payroll taxes (all social security payments and
payments to INFONAVIT). All employers with more than four employees are required to
use it. As of February 1998 all required employers are using the SUA.
*  Outsourcing of payroll tax collection function by IMSS. An agreement was signed
between IMSS and 12 special authorized banks (Entidades receptoras) relating to the
collection of payroll taxes and their transfer to the Banco de Mexico. Banks are required to
send the funds to the Banco de Mexico within 2 working days and the information to
PROCESAR within 3 working days (which will decrease to 1 day beginning in July 1998)
3.17   In the next several months, the authorities will focus on the following areas as part of
the fine-tuning of the system:
¢ Cutting costs. Currently the total costs for the centralized system is approximately 3% of
the flow into AFOREs, and it is shared by:
* IMSS: for affiliation, collection, and control
* Authorized Receiving Banks: for receipt of contributions and transfer of information
to PROCESAR
* PROCESAR: for processing, reconciling, and disbursing: The maximum
administration charge is NP$10.80 per account per year Circular CONSAR No. 14-1,
dated June 27, 1997) [Board Presentation 2h] Additional fees are charged for
specific operations performed by PROCESAR (affiliation, switching, etc.).
* Cutting time. Currently it takes approximately 10 days between the employers' payment of
contributions (t) and the actual arrival of the workers' funds into the AFOREs (t+1 0) to be
invested. Since PROCESAR is jointly owned by the AFOREs and the AFOREs start
collecting fees only when the funds are deposited in its account, there is an incentive for
PROCESAR to increase the efficiency of the system (Table 2 below).

3.19   The issue of cost cutting is particularly crucial in the case of Mexico, since the
contribution to the AFORE system is lower than in other DC schemes. Currently the average
worker's annual AFORES contribution (gross of fees) is estimated at about US$325/year (8.7%



20                                                                  PRESIDENT'SREPORT
of $312/mo2 - 6.5% plus 2.2% of the social quota). The average commission charged is
approximately 1.9% of flows, or approximately US$61 per account yearly. In even the rosiest
scenario - one consistent with rates at the lowest-cost institutional US mutual funds - a low-cost
AFORE might charge an administrative fee of $50 with investment costs close to zero. Even in
this best case, the low-wage Mexican worker would give up a substantial amount of his funds to
administrative costs each year, leaving him with only around $275 to invest in the pension per
year. Evidently such a low contribution level will not guarantee a very comfortable retirement,
even with reasonable real returns and a relatively long work period prior to retirement. The
minimum-wage earner would fare correspondingly worse.
3.20  Identifying opportunities for cost-cutting is merely a means to an end - namely, lower
administration costs increase the amount of funds that will eventually be available to pay benefits
to retirees (and their dependents). But because pension saving takes place over such a long time
period, it is useful to formulate a range of output standards when monitoring pension system
performance in the short and medium run. To this end, supervisory agencies charged with
oversight for pension plan behavior should delineate performance benchmarks regarding
investment returns as well as indicators of services delivered to system participants.
3.21   Since the Mexican pension funds now only offer a very standardized and limited
portfolio, and for the most part no differences in services, money management fees are the main
area where competition might occur. However, the commissions charged are difficult to
understand and compare. This is because the average plan participant faces a range of front-
loaded, asset-based, and return-based commissions that change in different ways through time
(Table 4). To make these comparable, one requires a model of contributions, returns, asset
accumulation, and holding periods.
3.22  CONSAR has finalized a study (October 1997) making these costs comparable [Board
Presentation 2i]. The study simulates eventual commissions paid and benefits received under
the different AFORES commission structures This requires assuming (i) a wage level and hence
a contribution level, (ii) a holding period, and (iii) a real rate of return and wage growth rate. In
the CONSAR figures, it is assumed that the hypothetical worker's pay is 3 times the minimum
wage (approximately the average pay of workers covered by the AFORES system), and real pay
growth is zero. Real annual investment returns in this simulation are alternatively set at either
3.5% or 5%, and the two holding periods considered are 25 and 40 years, respectively. These
computations also assume that the government's additional "social quota" is not included in the
contribution amount, and that the participant has a SAR accumulation of $4,000, immediately
transferred into the AFORES plan. Some illustrative comparisons of commissions charged under
the various AFORES are reported in Table 5 below.

contribution into individual SIEFOREs is approximately US$54.

3.23   Since pension systems produce a number of services in addition to money management,
CONSAR is currently developing a set of indicators permitting the government - and more
importantly the plan participants - to judge a plan's performance over time on non-financial
criteria. Performance standards should include indicators of how quickly and satisfactorily the
system responds to participant queries, how quickly and accurately taxes are collected and
invested, how long it takes claimants to determine eligibility for benefits and start receiving
checks, and how the system detects and corrects fraud. Related issues include how well the
system can acquire SAR92 fumds accumulated under the prior retirement system's arrangement,
and whether people add their own voluntary contributions on top cf the mandated sums.

A3. Transfer of SAR 92 balances
3.24     Transition workers have the right to transfer their accumulated contributions in the
retirement sub-account made between 1992 and August 1997 to AFORES. The AFOREs will
maintain these funds (SAR92) in a separate sub-account - invested in the same portfolio as the
SIEFORE. Workers have an acquired right to a 2 percent real rate of return on these funds for
the first year if they choose to maintain their balances in the Central Bank.
3.25     To ensure the smooth transfer of SAR92 accumulated balances from the Central Bank
to AFOREs, CONSAR (Circular 24-1, 12/10/97); SHCP (Oficio No.305, 12/10/97) and Banco
de Mexico have issued detailed processes establishing the means to authorize transfers and to
ensure that contributors are informed about the transfer [Board Presentation 3a]. TableA18 in
the Technical Annex describe in detail the SAR92 transfer scheme.

A4. In vestment Policies and Risk Management
3.28   The long-term sustainability of the pension reform undertaken in Mexico is critically
dependent on the investment returns that are obtained by the AFOREs and the financial
performance of INFONAVIT. CONSAR is responsible for the regulations governing the
investments by the AFOREs. The only constraint on CONSAR is that it can authorize



24                                                                                    PRESIDENT' sREPORT
investments only in domestic assets (the law does not allow international investments). The
investment regime governing the new pension system was issued by CONSAR in June 1997
(Circular 15-1, 6/30/97). Currently each AFORE is authorized to offer only one SIEFORE to all
its affiliates and the regulations governing the first SIEFORE are summarized in Table 7 below.

3.29 The actual investments of the AFOREs as of March 13, 1998 are provided in Figures 6(a)
and 6(b). The AFOREs are largely investing in government bonds (CETES, BONDES, and
UDIBONOS) and the limit on private bonds (issued both by banks and corporates) is currently
not binding. Due to requirement that inflation-indexed bonds form a large part of the portfolio,
the AFOREs are investing proportionately more than the share of instruments available in the
market in UDIBONOS. Less than 0.25% of the aggregate investments are in bank securities and
less than 1% of the aggregate investments are in other private debt instruments. The average
annual rate of return obtained by the AFOREs as of February 28, 1998 was 21.5%, implying a
real annualized rate of return of 6.2%.

activities have focussed on three issues: (i) the evolution of the investment regime; (ii) the
financial and market risks of the system and their management; and (iii) systemic problems that
could impact sound management of assets.
3.32 Regulations governing the investment regime. CONSAR has issued a number of
modifications and changes to the initial guidelines to address some of problems that arose once
the system became operational (Circular 15-2 dated 29 October 1997). [Board Presentation
4a]. The most important modifications made are discussed below.
*In vestments in Inflation-indexed bonds. A waiver was granted until the end of 1997 on the
earlier requirem-ent that at least 51% of the SIEFORE investments be made in inflation
indexed securities (the requirement that at least 65% of all investments be in governments
bonds continues). The reason for the waiver is that there were not enough inflation-indexed



26                                                                 PRESIDENT's REPORT
instruments for the SIEFOREs to invest in. It is expected that the Government's own
borrowing program from the market will provide these instruments over time.
*  Cash holdings. Earlier the SIEFOREs were not allowed to hold any cash balances. This
blanket ban on cash holdings created many operational problems for the SIEFOREs,
especially when there were any errors and omissions during the cash transfer process. In
order to simplify the process, the SIEFOREs are now allowed to hold up to NP $250,000 in
cash balances.
*  Repurchase Agreements. The requirement that SIEFOREs hold no cash also implied that all
the money received through contributions had to be invested immediately. This requirement
significantly constrained the investment plans of the AFOREs. In order to allow SIEFOREs
to better manage their cash positions and time their investments, CONSAR has issued
regulations allowing the SIEFOREs to invest in repurchase agreements or repos. The
amounts invested by a SIEFORE in repos is restricted to the lower of: (i) the amount
received in the most recent bimonthly contributions; and (ii) 10% of the overall assets under
management. For the next several months, the second constraint will be binding.
*  Asset Valuation. CONSAR earlier issued guidelines that the valuation of the asset holdings
of the SIEFOREs should be done on a daily basis using a vector of prices published by the
Bolsa de Valores - the Mexican Stock Exchange. It was soon discovered that the prices
received by the SIEFOREs and CONSAR were sometimes erroneous (and amended later by
the Bolsa on the following day) or sometimes were not available. In this situation, fresh
regulations were issued to clarify that the SIEFOREs were authorized to use the most recent
and accurate price vector available to report valuations.
* Others. CONSAR has clarified the earlier investment regulations so that the limits on
various investments should be adhered to at the time of investment. Changes in valuations
due to market movements do not constitute regulatory violations. This clarification is line
with international industry practice.
3.33  Risk management. In addition to providing the framework for the overall investment
regime, CONSAR also has responsibility to ensure compliance of the investment guidelines by
the AFOREs. CONSAR also needs to ensure the sustainability of the new system by getting a
measure of, and then requiring the AFOREs to manage the risks to which the investors' funds are
exposed. At present, there is a significant overlap between the compliance and risk management
functions of CONSAR. As a regulator, it is essential that CONSAR perform the compliance
function and provide regulatory procedures to resolve violations with guidelines. As regards risk
management, different participants in the system have responsibilities to manage different risks.
CONSAR should ideally focus on system-wide risks and the AFOREs should be held
accountable for portfolio and security level risks.
3.34 . CONSAR constituted two committees - the Comite de Analisis de Riesgos (the Risk
Analysis Committee) and the Comite de Valuaci6n (The Valuation Committee) to oversee its
operations in the risk management area [Board Presentation 4b]. Also, to achieve its
compliance function, CONSAR has put in place an extensive reporting system, which obtains
data from all AFOREs. The systems currently in place provide information to CONSAR about
the specific investments being made by the AFOREs. CONSAR is also developing systems that
would allow it to assess system-wide risks and allow it to identify potential trouble spots.



MEXICO - SECOND CONTRACTUAL SA VINGSDEVELOPMENTPROGRAM (CSDP ll)                27
3.35 In the area of risk management, issues remaining to be addressed include: (i) separating
CONSAR's compliance function from the risk management function, while focusing CONSAR's
activities on regulation and supervision; (ii) institutionalizing procedures to address systemic
risks - especially procedures to resolve AFORE mergers and bankruptcies; (iii) strengthening the
capacity of AFOREs to take on the risk management function internally; (iv) establishing
uniform standards of risk reporting by AFOREs; and (v) strengthening CONSAR's internal
capacity to oversee risk management function and provide institutional strengthening to
AFOREs, if necessary.
3.36  Broader financial sector issues. CONSAR also has a major role to play in addressing
some of the more miedium-long term issues in financial sector development in Mexico to resolve
some systemic problems with the objective of making the investment process more efficient and
effective. These aspects - which CONSAR is currently working on - include: (i) reducing the
time taken between the payment of contributions by workers and receipt by AFOREs; (ii)
improving the overall environment for investment by AFOREs; (iii) establishing a market
determined benchmark for performance evaluation and risk assessment of AFORE performance;
(iv) instituting a custodial system for AFORE and mutual fund assets; and (v) assisting in the
development of the corporate bond market and strengthening credit rating agencies.
A5. Supervision and Eforcement
3.37  Most outside observers, and many of the fund managers themselves, have judged
CONSAR's regulatory performance to be highly professional and effective. Within CONSAR,
the Direcci6n General de Inspecci6n y Vigilancia, a department that in 1997 accounted for
roughly 15.7% of the regulator's budget, is charged with the operational and financial
supervision of the new private pension system (See Table A14 in the Technical Annex for the
Organizational Structure of CONSAR). The department operates with a total staff of 55 and
carries out its supervisory function through remote, automated data analysis, validated by random
on-site inspections of the AFOREs. CONSAR has developed customized systems software that
feeds data on affiliation, the status of retirement accounts, SIEFORE investment activity, and the
eligibility of acquired instruments to the department's analysts on a daily basis. Financial data
on SIEFORE investment regulated by Circular 21-1, published November 11, 1997 [Board
Presentation 5a], establishes the methodology for the valuation of eligible investment
instruments.
3.38  The majority of supervisory functions are computerized, and operate in conjunction with
IMSS operating system. Since October 1996, with the formulation of a joint IMSS-CONSAR
working plan for the implementation and operation of the reformed system of social security,3 a
close working relationship has been maintained between the two agencies. Circular 16-1 and
20-1 published on June 30 and November 11, 1997 (see para 3.16) respectively, fornally link the
regulator's task of supervision of the AFORE system with the centralized collection
infrastructure established under IMSS and managed by PROCESAR. CONSAR's supervision
procedures are the only mechanism of daily financial surveillance that exists in the country, and
are supported by programs that perform crossehecks on data received electronically from the
AFOREs, PROCESAR and IMSS. CONSAR Circular 19-1 (11/17/97), specifies the data
3 Plan de Trabajo CONSAR-IMSS para la Implementaci6n Operativa de la Reforma a la Ley del Seguro Social



28                                                                    PRESIDENT' sREPORT
required by the supervision department from each of the actors in the pension system, a uniform
format and a strictly enforced schedule for their delivery. In addition, Circular 25-1 (12/11/97),
sets out the data and format specifications of the monthly reports required of each AFORE (see
para. 3.4).
3.39  Over the course of 1997, the department developed instruction and carried out training
programs for its staff in both off-site monitoring and on-site inspection. The bulk of these efforts
at building the supervisory capacity of the staff have been financed with World Bank technical
assistance. When staff monitoring operational and financial data detect AFORE activity outside
the regulatory framework, the offending fund manager is notified immediately, and if the
infraction cannot be satisfactorily explained or resolved remotely, an inspection team equipped
with portable computers linked to CONSAR's mainframe is dispatched to deal with the problem
on site. To date all the infractions that are detected have been dealt with in 24 hours.4 Staff has
noticed that imposing swift penalties on even minor infractions has rapidly established standards
for the industry. If one AFORE is disciplined, the others quickly correct their own activity in the
area of infraction. CONSAR's expanded technical capacity has accelerated response time,
reduced the amount of human resources necessary for supervision, and contributed to the
viability of the system.
3.40  CONSAR is required to report once every six months on the operation of the new system
to Congress. The first such report was prepared in February 1998 covering the July-December
1997 period. CONSAR will also provide statistical information on the new pension system in its
bimonthly bulletins. The bulletins will have a wide distribution and can be accessed from
CONSAR's web page. CONSAR emphasizes that the statistical bulletin will follow the system's
development by providing data on each contribution cycle, and that it is intended purely for
disclosure. CONSAR will refrain from any formal economic or financial analysis in this forum
that could be perceived or implied as an endorsement by participants in the market. The first
bulletin will be issued by June 1998.
3.41   Regarding challenges ahead, of the supervision department's fifty-five staff, only
seventeen are available to monitor the activity of all of the new market's participants. When
compared with the human resources dedicated to supervision in other Latin American countries
that have established similar systems of private pension fund management, this is relatively
meager. Also, the number of workers participating in the system is equal to that in Chile and in
Argentina combined, adding to resource demands. The department's task will be further
complicated by the eventual introduction of additional SIEFOREs per fund manager, and the
corresponding evolution of the investment regime. The supervision department will soon need to
upgrade its software to automatically perform notification and follow-up tasks that currently can
occupy up to two or three days of a supervisor's limited time. CONSAR has identified the
increasingly binding constraints on its supervision staff, and plans to considerably expand the
department's capacity to monitor complex financial transactions in order to ensure that as the
investment activity of the fund managers becomes more sophisticated, that the regulator is able
to keep pace.
4 The majority of infractions have been related to tardy or erroneous delivery of the daily, weekly, and monthly data
required by the regulator.



MEXICO - SECOND CONTRA,CTUAL SA viNGsDEvELopmENTPROGRAM (CSDP II)                  29
A 6. Disability and Life
3.42  Under the reformed system, the risk of disability and death is covered by two types of
insurance: workers' compensation (Riesgo de Trabajo - RT) and the life and disability insurance
(Invalidez y Vida - IV).  When a covered accident occurs at the workplace, workers'
compensation applies. When the disability or death are not work-related, the life and disability
(IV) insurance applies. The IV system requires a fixed premium to IMSS of 2.5% of private
sector workers' payroll. IMSS retains responsibility over the certification of this line of
insurance. Private insurance companies pay benefits through annuities to all cases beginning on
July 1, 1997.
3.43   Coverage for IV benefits is conditional on 250 weeks of contributions to the national
social security system, and there is no minimum age for eligibility. However if the disability is a
" major" one (with 75% loss of function) only 150 weeks of contributions are required. Benefits
under the disability pension are equal to 35% of the average covered wage the worker earned
over the 500 weeks of contributions prior to the disability, adjusted by the CPI. However the
benefit must at least be sufficient to equal the government-guaranteed MPG. Survivor insurance
benefits are equal to, 90% of the disability pension for a widow, and dependents receive 20% of
the disability pension (30% if both parents are deceased).
3.44 The worker seeking disability benefits must go through IMSS certification. In both cases,
workers' compensation and disability and life, once the certification is provided, IMSS has the
responsibility of comnputing the present value of the annuity flow payable to the disabled worker.
IMSS must pay the worker or his beneficiaries a sum insured (suma asegurada) equal to the
difference between the cost of an annuity (monto constitutivo), based on the benefits mentioned
above, and the accumulated balance in the individual AFORE account. With the sum of the
accumulated balances and the sum insured paid by the IMSS, the worker or his beneficiaries
must purchase the corresponding annuity from a private insurance company chosen by the
worker. If the accumulated balance is higher than the cost of the annuity, the worker or his
beneficiaries can withdraw the excess funds or increase the benefits of the annuity. In any case,
if the benefits are lower than the minimum pension the government will put up the difference so
that the worker can purchase an annuity with an income flow equal to the minimum pension.
These pensions are adjusted annually according to changes in the CPI.
3.45  The Government is currently focusing its reform efforts in three areas: (i) containment of
the costs of the IV scheme; (ii) design of the technical and operational mechanisms and the
regulatory framework for the smooth initiation of payment of annuities by the private sector; and
(iii) analytical work toward the fine-tuning of the system.
3.46 Cost containment. On June 18, 1997 IMSS and SHCP signed an agreement (Convenio de
Entendimiento) by which SHCP commits to pay for the disability pensions for current pensioners
while IMSS commits to a maximum increase in levels of disability cases ("siniestralidad') not
higher than 2.5% relative to 1996 levels. Also, SHCP commits to a medium-term financing plan to
make up for the difference between accumulated balances in the individual AFORE accounts and
the required amnount to purchase the annuity for the period in which AFORE balances will not be
sufficient. As part of this agreement, an SHCP-IMSS Committee was established to follow up on

the levels of disability pensions and any modifications in the expected flow of funds necessary to

Supervisi6n del Seguro de Pensiones) was established within CNSF [Board Presentation 6c].

A 7. Update of Fiscal Costs of the Transition
3.50   The full benlefits of a pension reform  are not felt unless the fiscal costs of the existing
unfunded system are made explicit to the extent possible and are funded through a credible
financing plan. As part of the design of the reform, the Ministry of Finance (SHCP - Secretaria de
Hacienda y Cr&dito Ptblico) developed in 1996 a model to 'estimate the fiscal costs of the
transition, including- sensitivity analyses under various assumptions. A detailed analy sis of these
costs, including an explanation of the model as well as sensitivity analyses under various
assumptions, is presented in Annex V of the MOP of CSDP I. SHCP has revisited these early



32                                                                               PRESIDENT'S REPORT
estimates in 1997 in light of updated macroeconomic and demographic projections, initial
implementation experience, the actual commission structure, and initial SIEFORE returns.
