3.52   The Government has completed the 1997 update to the sensitivity analysis of the fiscal
costs of transition and the evaluation of actuarial methodologies and the database [Board
Presentation 7]. Also, pension related transition costs for 1998 have been included in the 1998
budget. Figures 8a and 8b show that the 1997 revised estimates of transition costs are slightly



MEXICO - SECOND CONT4RACTUAL SA VINGSDEVELOPMENTPROGRAM (CSDP 11)                 33
lower than the 1996 projections. This results from: (i) higher GDP growth rates; (ii) changes in
biometric hypotheses, particularly disability and mortality rates; (iii) lower than projected
increases in the minimum wage - which has led to lower payments to current pensioners; and
(iv) lower actual cojmmission levels (on average, 1.5% compared to 2% for a 50 year horizon).
B.    INFONAVIT
3.53  Background. As the largest of Mexico's special housing funds, INFONAVIT is an
important source of mortgage loans and potentially mortgage funds in Mexico's housing finance
system. Along with its critical role in housing finance, the reforms undertaken in 1996 by the
Government to the social security system have given a key role to INFONAVIT for ensuring
adequate pension income through positive real returns for contributors.
3.54 Under the reforms, the 5 percent contribution of a worker's wage to INFONAVIT
represents between 30 and 40 percent of total contributions to pension accounts. Hence it is
important that these funds earn positive real rates of return. Other benefits from positive real
returns include: (i) potentially lower fiscal costs as the better performance of INFONAVIT
increases total returns on individual accounts and lowers the probability that younger transition
workers will choose the old PAYG benefits over the new system, or that the Government will
have to provide the minimum pension guarantee to new workers; and (ii) the higher potential
retirement income by workers just entering the new system which could reduce evasion or
provide incentives for workers to join the fornal labor force.
3.55  As can be seen from Table 9, INFONAVIT accounts for more than 38% of the supply of
mortgages in Mexico. In 1997, INFONAVIT granted nearly 100,000 mortgage loans with a
value of P$11.5 billion. INFONAVIT's total loan portfolio at the end of 1997 amounted to over
US$10 billion. Using the new collection system of the pension reform, workers' contributions
and loan repayments for 1998 are estimated to reach P$22 billion (US$2.6 billion).

3.56 CSDP I. Se'veral measures were introduced to improve INFONAVIT's financial
perforrnance. They include:
A strengthenedl supervisory role for the National Banking and Securities Commission
(CNBV). CNBVI has initiated a regular supervisory process using procedures applicable to



34                                                                   PRESIDENT'SREPORT
similar financial institutions. It is recognized that CNBV's role remains constrained since
according to the INFONAVIT law, CNBV's functions are limited to the approval of
INFONAVIT's accounting system and its internal audit function. It is empowered only to
report irregularities to the SHCP.
*  A stock taking of INFONAVIT's portfolio by external international auditors. As a first
step, CNBV commissioned an external audit and portfolio review of INFONAVIT (KPMG
was the selected auditor). The audit was completed in March 1998.
*  Greater public disclosure. The Government will disclose to the general public as well as
contributors in their individual account statements the rate of return on pension accounts,
including the AFORES and INFONAVIT. Greater disclosure has already raised public
awareness about the quality of INFONAVIT's portfolio and its impact on workers' savings.
*  Changes in the INFONAVIT law (Article 43 bis).  The revised 1997 law allows
INFONAVIT to undertake "virtual" cofinancing arrangements for mortgages through
commercial banks and other financial institutions.  This will subject a portion of
INFONAVIT mortgages to commercial pricing and standards to loan underwriting.
Since September 1997, the Bank has been working with the authorities in carrying out technical
analysis focused on the performance of INFONAVIT's mortgage instrument and the quality of
its loan portfolio.
3.57  Key issues. The 1995-96 reforms did not constitute a fundamental change in the way
INFONAVIT operates. In many ways, INFONAVIT's role and operations work against the
creation of a modern housing finance system and social housing policy. Key issues include:
*  Profitability of mortgage portfolio. Real rates of return have been negative in past years,
including 1997. The overall portfolio is weak, characterized by an excessive number of non-
performing loans estimated at between 46 and 62%.5 The poor quality of its asset base
requires provisions in excess of INFONAVIT's equity.
*  Portfolio management. Collection efforts by INFONAVIT of its overdue loans have been
minimal as it relies on large annual flows from workers' contributions to remain liquid.
Existing information systems are inadequate and cannot easily identify workers' loan
accounts or track payments into individual accounts that are identified. The systems also
cannot differentiate among workers that are not paying and are in arrears from those that are
evading or those that qualify for grace payment options while out of the formal sector. More
importantly, INFONAVIT's accounting and financial management systems do not
distinguish between loan principal and interest payments.
.  Nature of mortgage instrument. Loans from INFONAVIT have outstanding principal
indexed to changes in the minimum wage. Borrower loan payments are adjusted to a
maximum of 30 percent of a workers wage at interest rates which range from 4 to 8 percent
based on the worker's wage. The protection provided under INFONAVIT's mortgage
instrument goes to both relatively lower and higher income wage earners. Given its current
structure the instrument is not commercially viable nor securitizable as the minimum wage is
policy instrument not subject to market forces.
Because of the nature of the mortgage instrument the classification of performing and non-performing loans do not
have the same implications as in a commercial financial institution.



MEXICO - SECOND CONTRACTUAL SA VJNGSDEVELOPMENTPROGRAM (CSDP II)                35
*  Lending practices. Modeling INFONAVIT's mortgages shows that many of its loans are
too large for the purchasing power of its lower income borrowers or to older, higher income
borrowers. Credit criteria do not give enough weight to borrower payment ability.
*  Distortions to, housing finance and social interest housing policy. Excessive lending
amounts have resulted in directing lower income workers as well as developers away from
more affordable housing options. Such practices have contributed to market segmentation
and act as a disincentive to market-based housing finance solutions. The implicit subsides
provided have been ineffectively targeted.
3.58 Recent developments. The extent of INFONAVIT's problems as revealed by the
external audit, CNBV's supervisory efforts and the new disclosure policies has prompted the
Government to confront a long overdue restructuring of INFONAVIT's operations and finances.
Immediate concrete steps have been hampered by the lack of reliable loan account and income
recognition systerns. One of the first actions to be taken is for INFONAVIT to set aside
significant provisions amounting to more than P$30 billion. The size of the required adjustment
exceeded INFONAVIT's reserves and equity. After careful analysis of alternative actions,
CNBV was prepared to grant some supervisory forbearance provided INFONAVIT embarked on
a comprehensive reform and financial restructuring program over the next five years to return it
to financial soundness. These discussions with the Government and INFONAVIT culminated in
the recent signing of an intergovernmental agreement (the Convenio) dated March 27, 1998
signed by Secretary of Finance, the President of CNBV and the Director General of
INFONAVIT [Board Presentation 8a]. INFONAVIT's Board of directors approved the
Convenio on March 30, 1997. The Convenio was subsequently ratified by the Assembly of
INFONAVIT's shareholders on April 29, 1998.
3.59  Proposed Restructuring Program. The Convenio base represents a minimum set of
reforms that INFONAVIT must undertake immediately. The restructuring program covers the
following key areas:
Accounting reforms and prudential requirements. INFONAVIT has accepted new
accounting standards to be issued by CNBV which are consistent with the rules applicable to
commercial banks for mortgage loans (Circular 1343 and Article 1 of the Convenio) [Board
Presentation 8b]. CNBV has also required that INFONAVIT stop accruing and create
required provisions against interest and indexation on non-performing loans6[Board
Presentation 8c]. CNBV has adopted a new interpretation of INFONAVIT's law regarding
non interest expenses. With this new interpretation, current operating expenses will need to
be reduced significantly since they exceed the level permitted by law.
* Provisioning. CNBV has taken a conservative approach to provisioning. Under the
Convenio, INFONAVIT will provision P$30.4 billion, coming from: (i) existing provisions
(P$13.2 billion); (ii) equity (P$8.5 billion); and (iii) deferred provisions (P$8.7 billion)
[Board Presentation 8d]. CNBV permitted INFONAVIT to create a deferred asset to be
amortized over the next five years. If loan recovery improves, INFONAVIT will have to
demonstrate to CNBV's satisfaction that its financial performance justifies the reversal of
excess provisions. During the remainder of 1998 INFONAVIT will maintain parallel
accounting systems under its old and the new accounting standards. Beginning in January
6 Past due loans exceeding more than six months.



36                                                                PRESIDENT'S REPORT
1999 INFONAVIT will have to present its financial statements in full compliance with the
new accounting standards to be issued by CNBV.
*  Portfolio and individual account management. By January 1999 INFONAVIT will have
in operation a new portfolio management system designed to track individually service
performance of each loan, distinguishing between interest and loan repayments. It is
expected to trigger collection action and determine the quality of INFONAVIT's loans. The
new system will strengthen INFONAVIT loan origination activities and its financial
programming area by providing timely feedback on actual loan performance by type of
borrower. In addition, the new system will allow improved management of individual
accounts.
*  Changes in staffing. CNBV will maintain a permanent evaluation program to ensure
adequate staff and manager financial skills and compliance with agreed policies. CNBV can
recommend personnel changes should they be necessary.
*  Reengineering INFONAVIT operational processes. Based on the diagnostic work that
will be performed by external consultants and CNBV during 1998, INFONAVIT will start a
reengineering of its different operational functions, such as internal controls, loan approvals
authorizations, compliance with loan criteria and informational requirements, and loan
recovery actions. If needed, organization/structural changes would be started in 1999.
*  Loan recovery program. In lieu of opting for a good bank/bad bank approach INFONAVIT
will undertake a special loan recovery program with specific targets and timetable to be
strengthened, if needed, by outsourcing loan collection actions. The program will also track
delinquent borrowers who have exited the formal labor market.
*  Revamping of credit policies. Lending to high-risk groups which might entail losses to
INFONAVIT will be adequately provisioned against or discontinued by September 1998 at
the recommendation of CNBV. New credit guidelines will be developed and implemented
incorporating creditworthiness criteria, including adjusting the maximum amounts of credits
to reflect the borrowers ability to pay. The technical and financial standards used for
computing these amounts will be approved by CNBV. The new lending criteria, which will
give sufficient weight to favor borrowers with the capacity to make down payments, will be
presented to INFONAVIT's Board by no later than September 1998.
*  Reduction of operating expenses. INFONAVIT has committed to present a plan to reduce
its operating expenses gradually, starting this year, to comply with the law over a period not
to exceed seven years. The reduction in 1998 is agreed at about US$10 million.
*  Launching of pilot cofinancing program with commercial financial institutions. The
Convenio requires the management of INFONAVIT to propose the rules for this program to
its Board by July 1998. The rules proposed will have to be pre-agreed with these institutions
prior to their presentation.
*  Reform of treasury operations. INFONAVIT will establish an investment committee with
representation from professional financial experts from SHCP and SECODAM. New
policies and controls for managing INFONAVIT's liquid funds will be established. The
staffing and new procedures for this will be presented to INFONAVIT's Board, after prior
consultation with CNBV, by July 1998.
*  Revamping of Information Technology (IT) and Management Information Systems
(MIS). INFONAVIT will revamp its IT and MIS, focusing on the flow of information from
loan origination through loan recovery. The new systems will ensure linkages between



MEXCO - SECOND CON)TRACTUAL SA VINGSDEVELOPMENTPROGRAM (CSDP II)              37
various databases and a validation of historical data.  A full diagnostic study of
INFONAVIT's IT and MIS needs will be carried out along with definition of a time bound
implementation plan by December 1998.
3.60  Complementary Actions to the Convenio. Complementary actions will be implemented
in conjunction with the Convenio, including:
Strengthening governance. No changes to INFONAVIT's formal governance structure,
requiring legal modifications, are under consideration. The Government has taken or will
take a number of actions to address governance concerns. Among them are:
.  the approval of the Convenio by INFONAVIT's Board of Directors delegates
important decisions to CNBV, especially on the accounting, financial and personnel
fronts;
* CNBV's autonomy will be enhanced once new legislation is approved to transfer
control of its Board of Governors to the independent Central Bank;
*  SHCP is setting up a coordinating/steering committee for Government Board
members of INFONAVIT (SHCP, STPS and SECOFI) to ensure a coherent strategy
among such representatives at both Board and specialized committee meetings in
support of the reforms. The Government is also upgrading its representation at Board
meetings from the Director General to the Undersecretary level. A Monitoring
Committee made up of representatives from SHCP (Chief Advisor), CNBV (Vice
President) and INFONAVIT (Director General) will be established to review progress
weekly on implementing the Convenio;
* The Government intends to propose a policy of rotation of external auditors; and
* SHCP will prepare and implement a technical assistance program to carry out the
reforms and build institutional capacity.
-  Improving management capacity. Key appointments are to be reviewed by the Monitoring
Committee with the aim of selecting experienced and well qualified financial professionals
and managers. It is anticipated that in the course of the next few months the Government will
conduct an overall review, and further structural and organizational changes will be adopted.
*  Ensuring adequate returns to pensioners. INFONAVIT's law only requires INFONAVIT
to achieve a rate of return no less than annual changes in the minimum wage of Mexico City.
Since historically the minimum wage has lagged behind inflation, there is the possibility that
even financial performance complying with the law will yield a negative real return to
pensioners. Aware of his, the Government has agreed to use a benchmark indicator to
monitor this situation.
* Eliminating segmentation in housing finance. The Government's policy is to facilitate
INFONAVIT's current clientele that is bankable to obtain financing through the 43 bis
mechanism. This includes higher wage earners and borrowers eligible for the PROSAVI
program. Through the virtual cofinancing arrangement some INFONAVIT loans will be able
to utilize FOVI funding but benefit from direct loan repayment through payroll deduction
which is INFC)NAVIT's comparative advantage.
* Managing possible additional losses for 1998. Following the implementation of the new
credit rules, CNBV, with INFONAVIT, will review 1998 financial performance and require
additional provisioning, should it be necessary.



38                                                                 PRESIDENT'SREPORT
Defining enforcement plans. In addition to the powers granted to CNBV under the
Convenio, CNBV has expressed strong commitment to its enforcement, indicating that its
reputation as a financial sector supervisor is at stake. The signatories to the Convenio have
agreed that CNBV will withdraw regulatory forbearance if the terms of the Convenio are not
complied.
3.61  Compliance with the Convenio: CNBV's Implementation Arrangements. CNBV is
developing a set of financial performance indicators to follow up INFONAVIT's compliance
with the agreed clauses of the Convenio. Although it is still an ongoing exercise, the following
indicators are expected to be monitored: loan recoveries; past due loans; structure of loan
portfolio; operating expenses; leverage; loan-loss provisions; and financial performance.
3.62  Actions to be takenprior to CSDP II effectiveness include:
* establishment of an appropriate system of external financial controls beginning in 1999;
*  submission of a revised table of maximum credit amounts agreed with CNBV and approved
by INFONAVIT's Board of Governors; and
*  review of credit rules and loan rationing mechanism, and if necessary, submission of revised
rules to INFONAVIT's Board of Governors.
3.63 Bank Staff's assessment. A number of policy choices to reform INFONAVIT were
evaluated by the project team. From a technical perspective, introducing greater worker choice in
how workers' contributions in INFONAVIT are invested would have been the preferred policy
alternative along with deeper reforms which would change the institution's governance structure
and severely restrict its loan origination activity with the existing mortgage instrument and credit
rules. These reforms would have required important changes to INFONAVIT's law which, in
staff s judgement, were not feasible. The policy approach adopted by the Government through
the implementation of the Convenio could have a major impact on improving INFONAVIT's
operations and finances. It should be seen as a major step in the reform process which will lead
over time to the achievement by INFONAVIT of market rates of return for pension contributors
thereby bringing sustainability to this important component of the Government's social security
reform program.
C. Preparatory work for the Public and State pension systems
3.64  The 1997 reform did not contemplate reforms to the ISSSTE (Institute for Security and
Social Services for Government Workers - Instituto de Seguridad y Servicios Sociales de los
Trabajadores del Estado), PEMEX, other parastatals, and the state public pension plans. Hence, the
portability constraint across private and public sector workers' pension plans remains for now an
issue along with the financial disequilibrium to be faced in the future by these pension funds.
However, public sector workers only are approximately 2.5 million (Table 10). Thus, it should be
noted that the majority of covered workers (approximately 12 million) were included in the 1997
reforrn.

3.65   ISSSTE, thLe largest public sector scheme, administers - at the Federal level - retirement,
pensioners' healthi benefits, and disability pensions based on payroll contributions of 7.25
percent of wages for public sector workers. As is the case for private sector workers, additional
retirement income is derived from contributions to a specialized housing fund based on an
employer-paid contribution of 6 percent of wages for public sector workers administered by the
Government Workers Housing Fund (Fondo para la Vivienda de los Trabajadores del ISSSTE -
FOVISSSTE). ISSSTE population, compared to IMSS's, is older and more expensive.

3.66   In the preparatory work for the reform  of the public and state pension systems the
Government has advanced in the following two areas:
ISSSTE [Board Presentation 9a]:
The actuarial and financial diagnosis by an internationally recognized actuarial firm has
been finalized. The main conclusion is that the system is already facing a cash deficit,
estimated at P$3.7 billion in 1997 (approximately US$0.5 billion); P$4.6-4.8 in 1998, and
over P$6 billion in 1999. Contributions would have had to increase from 7.25% of payroll
to 14.95% to close the 1996 gap.
.  Given this financial disequilibrium  and the worsening demographics of the ISSSTE
population, the Government has initiated the detailed evaluation of the feasibility and the
fiscal transition costs of alternative reform strategies - supported by World Bank grant
financing.



40                                                                    PRESIDENT'SREPORT
States (32 pension schemes) [Board Presentation 9a]:
* In early 1997, the Government completed an initial survey of the available databases and
the actuarial and financial situation of the 32 public pension schemes at the State level. As
a result of this preliminary exercise, the Government decided to initiate an effort to institute
a minimum database in each State and to develop a standardized format for the actuarial and
financial valuation to be completed by all States.
*  During the first week of March 1998, the Ministry of Finance approved a standardized
format for the valuation (Proyecciones Demogrdficas y Financieras). These valuation
guidelines were designed through a joint effort of 7 actuarial firms. The Ministry of
Finance will provide the macroeconomic and financial hypothesis.
A Seminar is planned for early June 1998 to share with State representatives international
experiences on pension reform and the standardized formnat. This seminar will be
implemented with World Bank grant financing. The next step will be the application of
the standard format to the 32 State pension schemes.
D. Public Information
3.67 CONSAR is responsible for the dissemination of information about the new AFORE
system to the public. In the months leading to the system's start of operations the regulator
undertook a series of public relations efforts to educate workers on the premise behind the
reform, the mechanics of the system, and the role of its principal actors. CONSAR designed
information campaigns, ran advertisements on buses and billboards, recorded television and
radio broadcasts, released statements to the press, and closely monitored the content of
advertisements made by the AFOREs to ensure their integrity.' However, despite their efforts,
the authorities have been criticized for not communicating the fundamental changes to social
security brought about by the shift from the PAYG plan, to an individually capitalized, defined-
contribution system to the majority of Mexican workers. CONSAR is aware of the need for a
wider public education effort, but is constrained by the high price of radio and television
broadcast, that up to now, it has received at no cost by using non-peak airtime reserved for the
Government.
3.68  CONSAR has tried to bypass this constraint by designing information materials such as
brochures and video presentations that it latter disperses to the larger employers in the country
(employing 50 workers or more) and to job centers, in order to familiarize workers with the
benefits of the new system. Three separate promotional campaigns have been designed from
analysis of data gathered in worker surveys. Furthermore, in an effort to remain accessible to
employers, recently affiliated workers, and those considering affiliation, CONSAR maintains a
phone-in service to respond to questions and complaints. In addition, participants in the AFORE
system can go directly to CONSAR's headquarters to make their complaints in person: from
January to December 1997 CONSAR attended to the needs of 7,000 workers who visited the
regulator's headquarters. These efforts are being financed through the World Bank Financial
Sector Technical Assistance Loan (FTAL).
7 It should be noted that the bulk of fines levied on the AFOREs by the regulator in the months leading to full
operation of the new system, and shortly thereafter, were for misinforming the public.



MExico - SECOND CONTRACTUAL SA VINGSDEVELOPMENTPROGRAM (CSDP II)                41
E. Complementary Financial Market Reforms
3.69 The sustainability of the Mexican pension reform hinges on implementation of
complementary financial sector reform. As an integral part of the overall financial sector, the
performance of the pension system is linked to the health of the overall financial sector. The
Government recognizes this link and is undertaking a series of steps aimed at strengthening
various institutions in the financial sector. This section provides a brief overview of some of the
developments in the financial markets in Mexico. Annex V provides further details on the
reforms in these sectors.
El. Banking Sector
3.70 Mexico's financial system continues to be dominated by commercial banks and their
financial holding companies. These banks were hard hit by the devaluation of the peso in late
1994. The ensuing crisis in 1995 revealed and deepened the already poor asset quality in banks'
balance sheets. The Government has taken steps to restructure the banking system and increase
public confidence in the banking system, though at high fiscal costs (estimated at over 14% of
1997 GDP). The sector has been opened up to foreign investments, supervision and regulation
have been improved and universal deposit insurance (FOBAPROA) is now available. In addition,
several systemic issues are being addressed including the fiscal recognition of the costs of the
banking crisis.
3.71  In designing the new pension system, the Government took various steps to insulate the
AFOREs from the problems of the banking system:
*  The influence of the banks on the management of the AFOREs was minimized through
the requirement that all AFOREs be established as single-purpose separately capitalized
companies. T'he capital of an AFORE that is a subsidiary of a financial group is not available
to meet the obligations of other subsidiaries of the group.
* The extent of' pension fund investment in the banking sector was also limited by requiring
that SIEFOREs be permitted to hold a maximum of 10% of their assets in bank securities.
*  A regulation requiring that this 10% investment be made only in bank instruments with a
high credit rating (AA or above or equivalent by any of the three approved rating agencies)
ensures that the pension assets will be invested only in high quality instruments.
As of March 13, 1998, the AFOREs had invested an extremely small portion of their aggregate
portfolios (less than 0.25%) in bank instruments. The investments are in high quality short term
commercial papers. Hence, the limit of 10% of assets of each AFORE's assets is far from being
reached at the moment.
E2. Mutualfundts
3.72  As of January 31, 1998 the Mexican mutual fund industry consisted of 345 mutual funds
and 39 mutual ftnd operating companies, with total assets under management of $13.8 billion
with an investor base of about 321,000 investors. Almost $11 billion of assets are invested in
debt instruments and the balance in equities. The industry has been characterized by volatility in
both the number of investors and assets under management. The trend has been that individual
small investors have been declining and the funds have become more institutional. The number



42                                                                PRESIDENT'SREPORT
of investors has come down sharply since the high in 1993 and the industry is still recovering
from the impact of the 1994 peso crisis. The size of the industry, measured by the aggregate
amount of assets under management as a percentage of GDP, has been in the range of 3.2-3.4%
since 1995. Mutual fund shareholders in Mexican population is currently 0.3%, a fifth of the
population with bank accounts. This percentage has been gradually declining since the early
1990's. Performance data on an industry wide-basis along with an appropriate comparative
benchmark is unavailable.
3.73  The introduction of the private pension system has had a salutary effect on the regulation
of the mutual fund sector in Mexico. Recognizing the weaknesses in the regulatory structure for
mutual funds, the CNBV is currently considering reforms, which would bring the mutual fund
regulation more in line with the pension fund regulation. As part of the reforms, the CNBV is
considering the introduction of a mutual fund rating system along the lines of those published by
Morningstar in the US. These ratings are proposed to be done by the rating agencies.
E3. Voluntary pension plans
3.74  Several Mexican corporations have established voluntary pension plans for their worker's
over and above the mandatory social security programs. These schemes involve employee
contributions of between 2-6% with the plan assets managed by a bank or a brokerage firm.
These plans are essentially unregulated, although they are subject to certain broad portfolio
requirements by the SHCP. As part of the reform of the mandatory system, the new pension law
provided a framework to bring the voluntary pension plans under CONSAR's supervision.
CONSAR has recently issued regulations by which a voluntary pension plan can be registered
with CONSAR. Such registration is voluntary at the moment. Pension plans choosing to be
registered need to be certified as actuarially sound and maintain that status on an ongoing basis.
Participants of plans that register with CONSAR and subject themselves to its supervision have
significant advantages such as the ability to transfer their retirement accounts' at the AFOREs to
their private plan upon retirement and receive pensions under the terms of the voluntary plan. As
many voluntary plans provide pensions at an earlier age than the mandatory system, such
flexibility is attractive to the workers.
E4. Development of Long-Term Instruments and Financial Services
3.75  Long-term Government debt markets. Pension liabilities are typically long term and
the natural asset to hedge such liabilities is a long duration instrument such as long term bonds.
Mexico's Government bond markets are poorly developed and provide mostly short-term (less
than one year duration) bonds. Over 95% of all trades - both in the Bolsa and over-the-counter -
are in overnight securities. There is almost no liquidity in long-term instruments. While
historically, the cause of this underdeveloped state may have been the chronic inflation, it is
essential that now the Government considers development of this market segment.
3.76  Corporate debt market. The large presence of the banking sector in Mexico's economy
has traditionally implied that the level of intermediation has been very high. Most corporate
lending was from banks that in turn financed these assets from deposits. The presence of
institutional pension funds provides a natural alternate source of funds for high quality corporates
to raise funds. Development of a sound corporate debt market would provide another investment



MFUCO- SECOND CONTRACTUALSAVINGSDEVELOPMENTPROGRAM(CSDP II)                  43
vehicle for the pension funds. The investment regulations permit investment up to 35% in rated
private debt instruments. However, the actual investment by the SIEFOREs in private
instrumnents is very small (less than 1% up to March 13, 1998). Lack of adequate highly rated
corporate paper and the low risk adjusted returns on private paper are the main reasons for the
low level of investments in private securities.
3.77 Housing mortgage market. Long term securitized housing mortgages are a good
investment vehicle for pension funds, which have long-term liabilities. The availability of long-
term investors should also help to stimulate the housing market. However, the quality of the
investments depends to a large extent on the availability of appropriate instruments and
efficiency of the mortgage market. The Government is taking an active role in restructuring the
existing housing finance market. It is in the process of making the operations of FOVI more
market-oriented and the operations of INFONAVIT more transparent.
3.78    Financial Intermediaries. Credit rating agencies, custodial services, third-party
performance assessment services (similar to Morningstar and Lipper in the US) are some of the
other players in the overall financial infrastructure that are required for maximizing the economic
benefits of a privatized pension system. Mexico currently has three rating agencies, a central
depository that offers partial custodial services, and no independent performance assessment
agencies. The Gcvernment will examine these segments of the financial infrastructure and take
appropriate policy actions to assist in their development as part of the overall development of the
financial sector.
IV. BANK ASSISTANCE STRATEGY
A. Overall Assistance Strategy
4.1   Country Assistance Strategy (CAS). The loan is consistent with the CAS objectives of
growth, stability, and the modernization of the state set forth in the Mexico CAS (Report 16135-
ME) discussed by the Executive Directors in December 1996 and the recent CAS update
discussed by the Executive Directors on March 6, 1998. The proposed loan would directly
address the actions for reforming the pension system identified in the CAS and support several
other CAS objectives.
4.2   Other CAS objectives that will be supported by the proposed loan include growth, which
would be supported by (a) improving the incentives for employment in the formal sector by
lowering the burclen of payroll taxes; (b) increasing the efficiency of the pension system, thereby
reducing its distortionary effects on the economy; and (c) increasing long-term institutional
savings. Economic and social stability would be enhanced by improving the fiscal and financial
sustainability of the pension system, and by its contribution to social equity, a crucial element for
social and political stability. Modernization of the state would be enhanced since the reform
would aid Mexico in following other Latin American and OECD nations in reforming their
pension systems and guaranteeing workers a better retirement income.
4.3   Past Lending. The proposed loan represents the second operation in a series of three
one-tranche adjustment loans requested by the Government of Mexico aimed at supporting the
reform of its pension system and complementary financial sector actions. This is the product of



44                                                                PRESIDENT'S REPORT
several years of continued policy dialogue and sector analysis. Bank lending for pension and
financial sector reform in Mexico initiated in 1995 with the Financial Sector Technical
Assistance Loan (FTAL) and a Supplemental FTAL. These loans helped in the initial stages of
what later on became clear policy considerations to reform the pension system. Pension lending
began with the FY96 CSDP I (US$ 400 million) and, since then preparation of other related
lending operations was undertaken, specifically policy reforms in the health and housing sectors.
4.4   Future Lending. As indicated in the CAS, future Bank lending in the area of pension
reform is expected to incorporate a third Contractual Savings Development Program during
FY99/FYO0.
4.5   Experience with Adjustment Lending in Mexico.  The Bank experience with
adjustment lending in Mexico in the late 1980s and early 1990s has been mixed. Although most
of the development objectives supported by the operations were eventually achieved,
implementation lagged. Implementation delays were the results of the disparity between
expectations of quick adjustment disbursements and the deliberate pace in concluding sensitive
policy reforms. The main lesson from this experience is that adjustment operations should be
designed with broad-based Government commitment to policy reforms agreed with domestic
stakeholders and to carefully link disbursements to clear and significant policy reforms. Based
on the lessons learned, the proposed adjustment loan: (a) has been prepared by Mexican and
Bank teams in true partnership fashion, building on policy reforrns already underway, and (b)
will continue being supported by technical assistance (FTAL and Supplemental FTAL) which
closely track programs under the adjustment operation and provide timely technical input as
needed.
B. Coordination with the IMF
4.6   The policy reforms supported under this operation, and in particular the measures to
strengthen the performance of the private pension system - including the improved performance
of INFONAVIT - are congruent with the IMF's objectives in Mexico. Despite the lack of a
formal agreement involving the use of Fund resources, the IMF and the Government continue to
be in close consultations and are in general agreement over fiscal policy. The IMF has been
appraised of the progress of this operation and the policy reforms contemplated therein, and has
raised no objections.
C. Coordination with the IDB
4.7   Given the current economic situation, the IDB strategy for Mexico is divided into short-
and longer-term components. The IDB's short-term objective is to support Government efforts
to reestablish macroeconomic stability through (i) program lending aimed at assuring the
solvency of the financial sector and (ii) helping finance the basic needs of those most affected by
the crisis. To these ends, the IDB has cofinanced both of the World Bank's large crisis-related
operations: the Financial Sector Restructuring Loan and the Essential Social Services Loan. The
IDB cofinanced the earlier CSDP I with a US$300 million two-tranche adjustment loan.
Discussions relating to the disbursement of the second tranche (US$150 million) are ongoing.
4.8   The IDB's longer-term objectives are to support Government efforts to: (i) achieve
sustained economic growth, via infrastructure investments aimed at private sector development,
agricultural productivity growth, and human resources development; (ii) promote equitable



MEXICO - SECOND CONTRACTUAL SA ViNGSDEVELOPAMENTPROGRAM (CSDP II)               45
growth, through programs to reduce poverty and to improve the provision of social services
(education, nutrition, and health) --relying on and reinforcing the capacity of states and
municipalities and of the private sector; and (iii) achieve environmentally sustainable growth,
through programs geared to protect the environment and natural resources.
V.   THE PROPOSED LOAN
A.   Loan Size and Proposed Tranche Conditionality
5.1   Loan Size. A single tranche loan of US$400 million is proposed to be made available
upon loan effectiveness, anticipated for October 1998. The loan closing date would be June 30,
1999.
5.2   Proposed Tranche Conditionality. The policy actions below have been selected based
on their importance in the implementation of the new pension system and in the setting up of the
conditions for the expansion of the reforms to public sector workers. In addition to the specific
actions listed below, tranche release would be contingent upon: (a) maintenance of an
appropriate macroeconomic framework; and (b) satisfactory progress in the implementation of
the overall Contractual Savings Development Program which is outlined in the Government's
Letter of Sector FPolicy (Annex I).
5.3   The following policy actions were taken prior to Board Presentation:
1) Regulatory framework: CONSAR issued key regulations in the areas of: (a) disclosure
(para. 3.4); and (b) administration of individual accounts still in the Central Bank,
including (i) regulations on the outsourcing of information to workers and (ii) related fees
(para. 3.5);
2) Operation and efficiency of new pension system: (a) CONSAR has authorized
AFOREs in a timely fashion (para. 3.6); (b) CONSAR has issued general rules governing
affiliation contract (para. 3.7); (c) affiliation of at least 60% of potential IMSS's workers
(para. 3.7); (d) CONSAR has issued regulations relating to the switching among AFOREs
(para. 3.10); (e) CONSAR has issued regulations relating to the functioning of the
centralized collection and record-keeping system (para. 3.16); (f) generation of CURPs
for at least 25% of potential affiliates (para. 3.16); (g) the computerized reporting system
(SUA) was developed and implemented (para. 3.16); (h) CONSAR has issued regulations
on the maximum fees to be charged by PROCESAR for processing, reconciling, and
disbursing information on the administration of individual accounts (para 3.17); and (i)
CONSAR has finalized a study comparing commissions charged by AFOREs in a
standardized format (para. 3.22);
3) Transfer of SAR92 balances: (a) CONSAR, SHCP, and Banco de Mexico (Central
Bank) have issued detailed guidelines to make operational transfer of SAR92 balances
(para. 3.25); and (b) transfer of at least 20% of accumulated assets in SAR92 accounts at
the Central Bank have been transferred to AFOREs (para 3.26);
4) Investment policies and risk management: (a) CONSAR has issued necessary
modifications and changes to initial investment guidelines relating to investments in
inflation-indexed bonds, cash holdings, repurchase agreements, and asset valuation (para
3.32); and (b) CONSAR has established two committees to oversee its operations in the
risk management area (para. 3.34);



46                                                                 PRESIDENT'SREPORT
5) Supervision and enforcement: CONSAR has issued regulations establishing the
methodology for the valuation of eligible instruments (para. 3.37);
6) Disability and life (IV): (a) the Insurance Law was modified to incorporate the changes
to the provision of IV benefits under the reformed Social Security Law and to make
operational the provision of such benefits by authorized private insurance companies
(para 3.47); (b) CNSF issued a number of regulations to make operational the new
scheme (para 3.47); and (c) a Department in charge of supervision of pension-related
insurance (Direcci6n General de Supervisi6n del Seguro de Pensiones) was established
within CNSF (para 3. 47);
7) Update of fiscal costs of transition: the Government has completed the 1997 update to
the sensitivity analysis of the fiscal costs of transition and the evaluation of actuarial
methodologies and the data base (para 3.52);
8) INFONAVIT: (a) INFONAVIT, SHCP and CNBV signed an agreement on March 27,
1998, and approved by INFONAVIT's Board of Governors on March 30, 1998, to restore
INFONAVIT to financial health within a five year period through a major operational
and financial restructuring program (para. 3.58); (b) INFONAVIT has accepted new
accounting standards to be issued by CNBV which are consistent with the rules
applicable to commercial banks for mortgage loans (para. 3.59); (c) INFONAVIT has
stopped accruing, and created provisions against, interest and indexation of non
performing loans (para. 3.59); and (d) INFONAVIT has provisioned for its non
performing loans as required by CNBV and based on the findings of the external auditing
firm and CNBV's inspections (para. 3.59);
9) Preparatory work for the Public and State Pension systems (a) finalization of
actuarial and financial valuation of ISSSTE and initiation of detailed evaluation of
feasibility and fiscal costs of alternative reform strategies (para 3.66); and (b) finalization
of initial survey of data bases and actuarial and financial situation of 32 pension systems
at the State level and approval of standardized valuation format (para. 3.66).
5.4   The following policy actions on INFONAVIT would be taken prior to effectiveness:
(a) establishment of an appropriate system of external financial controls beginning in
1999 (para 3.62);
(b) submission of a revised table of maximum credit amounts agreed with CNBV and
approved by INFONAVIT's Board of Governors (para 3.62); and
(c) review of credit rules and loan rationing mechanism, and if necessary, submission of
revised rules to INFONAVIT's Board of Governors (para 3.62).
5.5   The third phase of the reform program is expected to focus on reforms in the relevant
institutions, in particular INFONAVIT, CONSAR, and the AFOREs to ensure the long-term
sustainability of the pension reform. Indicative actions are presented in para. 2.8 and Annex II.
The third phase is expected to be supported by a proposed third Contractual Savings
Development Program Loan to be presented to the Board towards mid-2000.
B.    Technical Assistance
5.6   The technical assistance requirements of this operation are being financed through the
Financial Technical Assistance Loan (FTAL) associated with FSRL I. In addition, a Policy and



MExCO - SECOND CONTRACTUAL SA VINGSDEvELOPMENTPROGRAM (CSDP HI)               47
Human Resources Development Fund (PHRD) in the amount of US$975,000 was approved on
July 26, 1996 to support the preparation of the second phase of the Contractual Savings
Development Program. A supplemental PHRD in the amount of US$975,000 was approved in
April 1998. Support through a technical assistance loan for INFONAVIT's restructuring
program is under discussion with the authorities.
C.   Disbursement and Auditing
5.7   Disbursements. The proposed second contractual savings program will be supported by
a World Bank adjustment loan of US$400 million, to be disbursed in one tranche. BANOBRAS
as the Borrower and financial agent for the loan would be responsible for submitting withdrawal
applications and would maintain separate records and accounts for all transactions under the
Loan. The Borrower will open an account in the Central Bank. Upon Bank notification of
tranche release, proceeds of the loans will be deposited by the Bank in this account at the request
of the Government. If after deposit in this account, the proceeds of the loan are used for
ineligible purposes (i.e., to finance items imported from non-member countries, or goods or
services in the standard negative list), the Bank will require the Borrower to either: (a) return
that amount to the account for use for eligible purposes; or (b) refund the amount directly to the
Bank, in which case the Bank will cancel an equivalent undisbursed amount of the loan.
5.8   Auditing. BANOBRAS will maintain separate records for all transactions under the
loan. Upon the Bank's request the Borrower shall have the deposit account audited in
accordance with standard Bank requirements.
D.   Lessons Learned
5.9   The main conclusions and principal lessons learned in the implementation of the first
phase of the CSDP (supported by the Bank's one-tranche CSDP I loan)8 are:
(i) The Importance of Borrower Ownership. As in any adjustment program, Borrower
ownership of conditionality under the loan is critical. Because of the Government's strong
commitment to reform of the pension system, the design, negotiation, and implementation of the
program were carefully carried out in a collaborative environment. Strong technical and political
leadership on the part of the Mexican authorities, complemented by a well qualified technical
team, provided a good context for taking full advantage of the Bank's advice and facilitated the
dissemination of lbest practice. The proactive nature of Borrower participation in the program
contributed to the credibility and the sustainability of the reform
(ii) Need for Sensitivity to Political Economy of Reform. The political economy surrounding
the formulation of labor policy has consistently constrained the reform agenda of even the most
resolute of governments. Social security reform is particularly treacherous, and especially so in
the midst of an election year, and a deep, although short-lived economic crisis. Political factors
and the legislative needs of the client need to be understood at entry, and should be included in
the Bank's dialogue with the client. In the case of the Mexican reform, the Bank's willingness to
lower its profile at the request of the authorities avoided the impact of domestic perceptions of
outside interference in the reform process.
' The ICR rates the outcome of CSDP I as highly satisfactory.



48                                                                  PRESIDENT'S REPORT
(iii) Selection of an Appropriate Instrument. Rather than risk delays and failure in the
achievement of the program's long term aims by locking the client into an overly ambitions and
politically sensitive policy agenda, the implementation team chose to negotiate a medium-term
reforn strategy, and to allocate its reform goals over a series of single-tranche adjustment
operations (STOs) - a lending vehicle design by the Bank specifically to protect the integrity of
lengthy adjustment operations from significant political shifts in borrowing countries. The
Bank's choice of the STO over the traditional multiple-tranche option provided the Bank with
greater agility in the disbursement of the loan, and enhanced efficiency and leverage in the
formulation of policy actions under the proposed second loan. So far the decision to agree on a
medium tern reform strategy supported by well focused STO's appears to be an innovative and
tactically appropriate response, suggesting that new Bank products can make the institution a
more effective partner in the development process.
(iv) Close Cooperation with Cofinanciers. Coordination with the IDB was continuous and
fruitful for both institutions and the client. However, in joint adjustment programs it is essential
to maintain highly compatible if not identical conditionality between the cofinanciers.
Conflicting conditionality can send mixed signals to the Borrower.
(v) Consistency in Objectives and Coordination between Bank Operations. In the course of
preparing and implementing complex sector operations, it is essential to orchestrate the
objectives of related Bank projects in order to ensure consistency and the achievement of
desirable outcomes. In implementing the first phase of the Contractual Savings Development
Program, and the complex financial sector adjustments required under CSDP I, both the Mexican
authorities and staff at the Bank relied on extensive work carried out under the preceding FSRL
and FTAL. Additionally, preparatory work for CSDP II related to reform of INFONAVIT, has
been carefully coordinated with the bank's team working on housing finance.
E.    Monitoring and Reporting
5.10  Monitoring and reporting will be facilitated through the close coordination of activities
and implementation agencies supported by the FTAL and technical assistance from the Bank.
Under the FTAL, technical assistance is being provided to assist the Government in
implementation of the overall program through CONSAR, CNSF, and SHCP.
F.    Benefits and Risks
5.11  Benefits. As with the CSDP I Loan, the implementation of the Government's contractual
savings development program, supported by CSDP II, will result in a number of important benefits:
a.  The change from an actuarially bankrupt defined/benefit system to a fully funded
defined-contribution scheme will increase public savings in the longer term. If the
current system were maintained, eventually the actuarial deficit would have to be met
with a subsidy from the fiscal account, an increase in contributions, or a decrease in
benefits. Analytical work on pension reform for public sector and state employees will
review options and help estimate the costs of moving to a financially sustainable
pension scheme for these workers.
b.  The separation of the pension and health sector schemes will result in greater pressure
to improve efficiency in the delivery of public health services. The efficiency of health
services delivery must improve to avoid an increase in contributions or a cut in
benefits.



MEWGCO - SECOND CONTRACTUAL SA VINGSDEVELOPMENTPROGRAM (CSDP /1)                  49
c. The reform has created a new class of institutional investors (AFORES) and an
increased volume of institutionalized saving. As institutional investors gain expertise
in managing pension funds, the allocative efficiency of domestic savings will increase.
In addition, institutional investor demand for long-term assets permits the possibility
of the development of markets for long-term financial instruments.
d. To the extent that institutional investors hold bank-related financial paper, a likely
outcome in the near term, these investors can play an important role in monitoring and
controlling bank risk by avoiding the paper of risky banks.
5.12  The pension reform is expected to have a positive social impact. Low-income formal sector
workers rely heavily on publicly provided pensions for sustainable post-retirement income
generally due to an insufficient accumulation of other savings. The establishment of SAR accounts
in the first phase of reforms in May 1992 provided many individuals with their first account with a
commercial bank. The Government's reform program is expanding access to financial services to a
broader base of the population and at the same time provides greater incentives for informal
workers to join the formal economy. Specifically, the implementation of the reforns supported by
the proposed operation will allow individuals to accumulate personal wealth, benefiting from
higher real rates of return in a broader range of investment vehicles improving post-retirement
living standards. The public information campaign being carried out has gradually increased
awareness of the benefits of the new pension scheme and change current perception so that
contributions are jperceived to be linked to benefits and an accumulation of wealth rather than as a
tax.
5.13  As workers retire under the reformed system they will buy annuities to convert the assets
accumulated in their pension accounts to yearly income to provide for retirement, which will
require the development of a regulatory framework for a private annuity market. This already
underway in the disability and life insurance areas. The growth of a private insurance industry will
create a broader irLstitutional investor base, which will support development of capital markets.
5.14  Risks. There are several major risks which could limit the effectiveness and impede the
success of the proposed pension reform program:
a.  slippages or inadequate supervision of private pension fund managers;
b.  the weakening of the banking system adversely affecting the reformed pension system;
c.  difficulties in establishing unique identification numbers for contributors and in
reconciling multiple accounts which would delay the implementation of the pension
reform.
d.  inadequate improvements in INFONAVIT's financial performance;
e.  slower-than-expected development of a long-term securities market which would reduce
the investment options of pension funds, potentially constraining their returns; and
f. lack olf sufficient fiscal discipline necessary to maintain a favorable macroeconomic
environment.
5.15  The track record to date on the first risk is very encouraging. CONSAR has been judged
to have effectively carried out its regulatory responsibilities. CONSAR has been and will



50                                                                  PRESIDENT'SREPORT
continue to be conservative in permitting new funds with different risk profiles while to
continuing to build its capacity.
5.16  To prevent the weaknesses of the banking system from adversely affecting the reformed
pension system, the Government's CSDP includes specific safeguards with respect to the
investment by AFORES in commercial bank liabilities. In addition, the Government has initiated a
second stage of banking reforms to ensure the restoration of the health of the banking system.
5.17  To preserve competition and reduce extreme reliance on financial conglomerates, the
Government has tried to facilitate broad entry and limit regulatory barriers in the establishment of
pension fund operators to stimulate competition. Limits included on foreign participation in
pension fund operators are only partial. In addition, these limits do not apply to a number of
countries with whom Mexico has reached agreements, including the U.S. and Canada under North
American Free Trade Agreement (NAFTA). Of particular importance have been Government
actions to permit each investment fund account administrator to sub-contract services freely in such
areas as collections and account processing.
5.18  Progress to date has been satisfactory in instituting a unique identification number for each
account holder and reconciling accounts for approximately 12 million contributors.
5.19 The Government recognizes the importance of improving INFONAVIT's performance to
ensure better retums for workers and minimize fiscal costs resulting from the minimum pension
guarantee. A strengthened supervisory presence by CNBV under the recently signed Convenio is
expected to help set the stage for a serious restructuring of INFONAVIT's operations and finances.
5.20  The risk of a slower-than-expected supply response for a variety of long-term securities is
being mitigated by: (i) reasonable prospects for macro-economic and monetary stability; and (ii)
improvements in the legal/regulatory framework for securities markets and mutual funds.
5.21   Finally, the potential for weakening of fiscal discipline will persist but is mitigated by both
Mexico's growth potential over the medium term and the significant amount of time remaining in
the term of the current administration which is strongly committed to these reforms. In addition,
the cost of the pension reform at between 1 and 2 percent of GDP has been adequately estimated
and is manageable. The low overall level of pensions and the favorable demographic structure in
Mexico are important factors which are helping to minimize the fiscal costs of the transition
compared with other countries in the region. Sensitivity studies using different rates of return and
actuarial estimates indicate that the range of costs fall within the scope of recent fiscal efforts and
are achievable both in the short and long term.
G.   Program Objective Categories
5.22 The loan belongs to the category of Economic Management by helping the Government
develop policy and tools to limit the fiscal impact of pension reform and stimulate growth in the
formal employment sector. The loan will also have long-term effects in Private Sector
Development by opening avenues for true private sector competition in the management of

retirement savings. Poverty Alleviation would also be supported through the extension of social
security coverage to informal sector workers and the self-employed.
5.23  Environmental Aspects. The program is not expected to have any environmental
impact; it has been thus rated C.
VI. RECOMMENDATION
6.1    I am satisfied that the proposed loan would comply with the Articles of Agreement of the

James D. Wolfensohn

MEXICO
SECOND CONTRACTUAL SAVINGS DEVELOPMENT PROGRAM LOAN (CSDP II)
GOVERNMENT LETTER OF SECTOR POLICY
FOR
THE SECOND PHASE OF THE CONTRACTUAL SAVINGS DEVELOPMENTPROGRAM1
Mexico City, May 13, 1998
Mr. James D. Wolfensohn
President
World Bank
1818 H Street, N.W.
Washington, D.C. 20433
EXC 85120 Room E 1227
Dear Mr. Wolfensohn:
With regard to the loan requested from the World Bank in support of the Second
Contractual Savings Development Program, I would like to draw your attention to a number of
relevant developments in the Mexican economy and the Government's macroeconomic policy,
and in particular to steps taken and to be taken to reform the country's pension system.
In 1997, the Government's economic policy objective was to consolidate the recovery
process begun in 1996 while maintaining the decline in inflation, and to do so in a context of
market stability conducive to the mobilization and increase of savings and investment flows.
The year saw clear progress in consolidation of the country's openness to trade, in the strength of
its public finances, in the prudent management of its monetary policy, and in the sound financing
of its current account deficit.
Against this background, gross domestic product (GDP) grew by a real 7% in 1997, a
figure not seen in the previous 16 years. This expansion led to the creation of new sources of
employment, with the result that open unemployment fell to 3.2%, or almost half the level
recorded at the high point of the crisis. Great strides have also been made in bringing down
inflation to 15.7% for the year, or to less than a third of the figure for the previous two years.
The external debt strategy that was followed resulted in a reduction of US$10.4 billion compared
to 1996.
In 1997, public finances were managed with strict fiscal discipline. Government
expenditure increased by 6.7% in real terms, and was financed chiefly from increased taxation,
both oil and non-oil. The public sector primary balance showed a surplus of P$ 110.1 billion,
equivalent to 3.42%/o of GDP. This resulted in a general balance deficit of P$24.5 billion,
equivalent to 0.76% of GDP. The current account showed a deficit of 1.8% of GDP, amply
covered by unprecedented inflows of foreign direct investment, totaling close to US$12.1 billion.
' Unofficial translation from the original Spanish text.



ANNEXI
Page 2 of 4
It is important to mention that as a result of its open trade policy instituted several years
ago, Mexico today possesses a dynamic external sector, which has been a crucial element in its
recovery. The total value of the country's foreign trade was approximately US$220 billion,
equivalent to over half its GDP, making it the tenth-ranked exporter in the world and the first-
ranked in Latin America.
On the basis of its progress in 1997, Mexico has set the following primary economic
policy objectives for 1998: (a) to create the conditions which will ensure that the expansion in
productive activity and employment is reflected in higher income levels and living standards for
the population at large; and (b) to continue laying firm foundations to support sustainable growth
in productive activity and employment over the medium term, in a context of declining inflation.
Success in achieving these goals requires an ample and stable pool of savings from which to
finance the necessary investment, ongoing moves to increase the efficiency of the production
apparatus, and renewed efforts in the campaign against poverty.  Consolidation of the
stabilization process will also be fundamental to that success. Continued sound management of
public sector finances and application of a monetary policy geared to low inflation targets, will
also be essential in promoting stability and reducing the inflation rate permanently.
Despite the less favorable character of the international environment owing to the
anticipated decline in the US economic growth rate, the adverse effects of the crisis in Southeast
Asia, and the fall in oil prices, Mexico's expectations for 1998 are close to 5% real economic
growth, and achievement of a 12% inflation target.
The public sector deficit is expected to be equivalent to 1.2% of GDP, and the current
account deficit to be in the approximate range 2.5-3% of GDP. Projections indicate that the latter
will be covered by long-term external savings, including resources drawn from international
financial agencies like the World Bank.
In order to ensure that the goals set for the 1998 economic program are achieved, the
national budget has been cut back on two occasions to bring it into line with the reduction in
projected revenue from oil exports following the drop in oil prices. As part of the process
Mexico is currently going through, and in order to create the conditions that will help generate
the domestic savings required to finance national development, the Government has instituted a
series of reforms to the pension system.
Besides giving workers the certainty of having adequate savings for their retirement
years, these reforms will create a broad base of domestic long-term savings that the country can
draw on to finance a range of productive activities that require long maturation periods, develop
its own capital market, and promote cost-effective projects.
The first phase of the Contractual Savings Development Program (CSDP), together with
the technical and financial support provided by the international financial institutions, especially
the World Bank, contributed materially to the implementation of major reforms in this sector.
The primary objectives of CSDP were to increase the equity, efficiency, and long-term
sustainability of the pension system, gradually expand its effective coverage, and limit the fiscal
impact of the reforn process.



ANNEXI
Page 3 of 4
In December 1995, the Mexican Congress enacted legislation that replaced the former
pay-as-you-go system with a new system of individually funded investment accounts.
Subsequently, the April 1996 Retirement Savings Systems Act established the structure and
delineated the powers of CONSAR, the National Retirement Savings Commission. In December
1996, the legislation establishing INFONAVIT was amended to adjust that agency's operations
to those of the pension system and to lay the foundations for joint collection arrangements with
the Mexican Social Security Institute (IMSS). In addition, provisions were introduced for a
mechanism to facilitate cofinancing with banks and limited-purpose financial corporations
(SOFOLEs).
CONSAR oversees the proper functioning of the pension system, and issues general
guidelines governing the operation and supervision of AFOREs and SIEFOREs. Its general
mandate is to ensure that workers' resources are invested safely and generate adequate returns.
On June 30, 1997, CONSAR issued rules prescribing in clear terms the investment
regime with which all SIEFOREs must comply. Their portfolios are required to consist
essentially of securities that maintain the purchasing power of workers' savings.
In addition, CONSAR requires that workers receive regular disclosure on such important
aspects of the new system as SIEFORE investment returns, commissions and fees, and the
balances in their individual accounts.
The data obtained so far from indicators of the performance of the new system warrant a
conclusion that the initial objectives set have been accomplished fully, and in some cases
exceeded. At the present time, a total of 17 AFOREs participate in the market; they receive
worker and employer contributions for over 12 million workers and have close to P$25 billion
under management. These resources consist only of funds deposited in the old-age and
severance insurance sub-account. The efforts made have produced results, and have laid the right
foundations for generating domestic long-term savings.
The Government will continue its efforts to broaden, deepen, and consolidate reform of
the pension system, to strengthen CONSAR's supervisory capacity and to obtain better returns
on funds deposited in the housing sub-account managed by INFONAVIT. It is against this
background that Mexico is seeking World Bank financial support to advance the second phase of
the Contractual Savings Development Program (CSDP II), the objectives of which are to
guarantee more comprehensive social security.
One of the major aims of CSDP II is to strengthen the capacity of INFONAVIT to
produce positive real returns on the worker resources deposited in the housing sub-account. To
that end, CNBV, the National Banking and Securities Commission, exercising its powers under
the law and acting on the best available estimates of INFONAVIT's past-due portfolio,
recommended in March 1998 that the institute set aside additional contingency reserves in excess
of its net worth. After reviewing INFONAVIT's financial situation, CNBV granted it regulatory
facilities to enable it to establish the required reserves over time, providing it put in place
remedial measures that would guarantee its financial viability.



ANNEX I
Page 4 of 4
At its meeting on March 30, 1998, the board of directors of INFONAVIT ratified an
agreement (the "Convenio") between itself, the Finance Ministry (SHCP), and CNBV, that spells
out in detail the measures indicated above and specifies the dates by which they are to be put into
effect. The General Meeting of INFONAVIT, its highest governing body, ratified all clauses of
this agreement at its session of April 29, 1998, instructing the institute's management to report to
it at regular intervals on progress with the implementation process.
SHCP and CNBV will be in charge of ensuring strict compliance with the Convenio. The
restructuring program includes reforms in INFONAVIT's administrative structure, operations,
and credit policies.
Another key aim of CSDP II is to consolidate the powers that enable CONSAR to carry
out its mandate as the regulatory authority over the pension system. In the next few years the
Government's primary interest is in seeing CONSAR exercise absolute oversight authority not
only over the AFOREs and SIEFOREs, but also over all other actors in the system.
The main components of the second phase of the Contractual Savings Development
Program will be:
*     implementation and consolidation of reforms to the new pension system covering
private sector workers;
*     updating of estimates of the fiscal cost of the reform;
*     support for INFONAVIT's reform efforts to improve its operating efficiency and
thereby raise the yields workers earn on their housing sub-accounts;
*     progress with preparations for reforms to strengthen the pension systems covering
workers in the public sector;
*     continuing information and education campaigns to increase the public's
knowledge and understanding of the pension system;
*     implementation of complementary financial market reforms.
Implementation of these measures is expected to have a favorable impact on the
operations and functioning of the new pension system, and thus to contribute to Mexico's social
and economic development. Consolidation of reform of the pension system is a major priority in
the Federal Government's development strategy, and support from the World Bank in reaching
that objective will be invaluable.
Please allow me to take this opportunity to express our appreciation to the World Bank,
and to thank you as its President for the organization's willingness to support the advance of the
second phase of Contractual Savings Development Program in Mexico.
Yours truly,
Angel Gurria
Secretary

a. Under IVCM contributions could not exceed 10 times the minimum wage and under the new system it is 25 times.
b. Plus the accumulated balances in the retirement sub-account (from 1993 to 8/1997) if the worker so wishes.
c. Government contribution of 5.5% of one minimum wage (MW) - in July 1997 - per day is indexed to the consumer price level. On average,
this quota will be equivalent to 2.0 % of wages (considering that the average wage is approximately 2.6 times the minimum wage).
d. Lump withdrawal at retirement permitted only for balances in excess of 130 % of the cost of an annuity equal to the MPG.
e. Gradual withdrawals will consider insurance to cover probability that they live longer than expected and outlast savings.
f. Currently average wage for IMSS affiliates is 2.6 minimum wages, thus MPG is approximately 38 % of average wage.

Notes:
a. Contributions to the old system cease on August 31, 1997. Transition workers can choose at retirement the higher of the benefits available under the old PAYG scheme or the new defined
contribution plan.
b. "Compensatory pension" is paid upon retirement, not as a bond. The value is based on years of contribution and last ten years' eamings.
c. Workers with fewer than 150 weeks of contributions are not eligible for a recognition bond.
d. Only workers who have contributed for at least 48 months in the last 10 years and have at least 6 months prior to entering the new system.
e. Disclosure of expected current and future fiscal costs would be made on an annual basis.
L MPG introduced with second round of reforms in 1995, but regulations have not yet been issued.
g. This is paid by the employer.
h. Social Quota indexed to the CPI and estimated between I and 5.5 percent depending on worker's income and on average equivalent to 2.2 % of wages.
i. The rate shown is for both private and public sector and for 1996 and following years. Contribution rate increased gradually during 1994-96.
j. Different methodologies used. Source for Colombia and Chile is Schmidt-Hebbel, 1995 (page 58); for Peru is Kane and Palacios, 1996 (page 38).
Sources: World Bank, 1994 (page 277); Diaz Ortega, 1996; and World Bank intemal documents.
k. Current pensioners continue to receive benefits from the National Treasury as defined under the old system
1. Except those workers eligible for retirement under the rules of the old system by April 30, 1997. The AFPs began operating on May 1, 1997.
m. Affiliates who have contributed to the old system will receive compensatory pensions, paid monthly upon retirement, and calculated for those having contributed at least 5 years according to an
actuarial formula, von Gersdorf 1997 p. 9.
n. Law 1732 does not provide for a govemment guaranteed minimum pension. However, revenue from Bolivia's privatization (capitalization) of state enterprises is managed by the new system's AFPs
and has been earmarked for the 'Bonool" program ,by which every citizen over 65 is eligible to receive an annuity of US$248 in addition to the required annuity of at least 70% of the minimum wage,
from their accumulated pension assets. Citizens will continue to receive the Bonosol annuity after the savings in the individual accounts have run out.
o. An additional 13% of wages are withheld: 10% contribution for health insurance, and 3% contribution to the housing fund.
p. Of the 12.5% of workers wages withheld by the employer, 2.0% is an insurance premium covering physical impairment or death from common causes, 0.5% pays for the AFP services, and
employers contribute an additional premium of 2.0% for work-related injury or death.
q. Before the reform, most formal sector workers in Bolivia, private and public, belonged to practically obligatory employer-arranged plans, complementary to the PAYG plan. Contribution rates
under these plans varied from 3.5% to 12%.
r. Pension law specifies that the maximum limit on investment in foreign securities will be no less than 10% and no more than 50%, and only in instruments traded on the NYSE and the LSE. For the
first 15 years of the system, the AFPs are required to invest up to US$180/year in treasury bonds, to help finance the fiscal costs of transition.
s. Only those eligible for retirement at the end of 1996 will continue to receive benefits according to the old PAYG actuarial formula, paid out by the Banco de Previsi6n Social. A transition PAYG
system is set up to ease shift to the new two-pillar, PAYG/Capitalized system. See President's Report No. P7190-UR, for details.
t. At the present stage of the reform process in Uruguay, the total contribution rate to the pension system remains at 27.5%, however, employee/employer share of contribution has changed, and earned-
income ceilings have been put in place, based on May 1995 pesos. For details on the reformed contribution structure, see Table 1. I in Annex II of PR No. P7 190-UR.
u. The salary on which the public pension is calculated, under the new system, will be subject to a ceiling of 5,000 pesos/month (May 1995 pesos). The formula for calculating the public pension is
500/o of the average salary of the last 10 years.
v. Domestic equity is allowed for AFAP investment in Uruguay, but because of political difficulties in privatizing parastatals, equity instruments are scarce and capital markets are thin. AFAPs are
barred from investment in foreign equity.

Table A10
5I    CenraizedCollection System
Fkac
I~FONA                       Mhi*hy
(SHM~~~(S 
(2) ~~~      17 PROCESAR                          si-  q g ai
1  (6)               (5)1       /(1)$[T+
F                                     T
I ENPLOYfERS I          No        h 9    
| (3)[jad I                    (4) d
I = Flow of h-fomiation
$ =Flowof-Cmributiori
(1) IMSS sends information electronically to PROCESAR and INFONAVIT on invoices
issued.
(2) IMSS issues an invoice (with information from INFONAVIT) to employers, including all
Social Security and Housing Fund contributions for each of their employees.
(3) The employers determine their obligations using either the SUA (Sistema Unico de
Autodeterminacion) or the statement issued by the IMSS, and pay at the authorized
receiving banks.
(4) The authorized receiving bank deposits all collections received into the Central Bank of
Mexico (Banco de Mexico).
(5) The Central Bank of Mexico sends electronic information to PROCESAR on all
transactions (deposits received).
(6) The authorized receiving banks electronically send information on employer payments to
PROCESAR.
(7) PROCESAR assigns contributions to individual workers' accounts and informs the
respective AFORE.
(8) Once the amounts are confirmed by the AFOREs, PROCESAR requests the transfer of
resources from the Central Bank of Mexico to a commercial bank, who then transfers the
money to the AFOREs.
(9) PROCESAR calculates the social quota and government contribution and requests that the
Minister of Finance (SHCP) make the corresponding deposits in the Central Bank of
Mexico. Once completed, follows same process as step (8).
(10) At the request of PROCESAR, the Central Bank of Mexico transfers the resources to a
commercial bank.
(11) The same day the resources are received, the commercial bank transfers the resources to
each of the AFOREs.
Notes
1/ Entidades Receptoras
2/ Banco Liquidador
