Annex IV
Page I of 10
MEXICO
SECOND CONTRACTUAL SAVINGS DEVELOPMENT PROGRAM
INVESTMENTAND RISK MANAGEMENTIN THE REFORMED SYSTEM
1.    Introduction
This Annex focuses on three aspects of the reformed pension system in Mexico - (i) the investment regime
and its evolution; (ii) the financial and market risks of the system and their management; and (iii) systemic
problems that could impact sound management of assets. The discussion in Section 2 on the investment
regime covers past regulations that have been issued by CONSAR, analysis of problems arising thereof and
subsequent corrective steps taken by CONSAR. This discussion deals with the evolution of the regulatory
environment. Section 3 addresses the role of CONSAR in managing risks in the new system. It discusses
issues in the identification, measuring, monitoring, and management of market and systemic risks, credit
risks, and liquidity risks. The section also highlights the importance of an integrated approach to risk
management by CONSAR. Section 4 deals with broader systemic issues such as valuation, market
benchmarks, performance reporting, diversification into new asset classes and institutional strengthening
and their implications for system efficiency. In all these areas, the initial performance of the system in its
first few months in operation is assessed and recommendations for future actions based on international
best practices in order to put the new system on a sustainable basis are presented. It should be noted that
the sustainability of the reformed pension system as a whole depends crucially on the financial performance
of INFONAVIT. A large share (about 40% for a worker earning the average wage, which is about 2.8
times the minimum wage) of social security contributions go to INFONAVIT.
2.     The investment regime
The Ley de los Sistemas de Ahorro para el Retiro - the governing legislation for the reformed system
enacted in April 1996 - provides general principles relating to the investment management regime. Article
43 of the law authorizes CONSAR to establish and enforce rules of general application for the composition
and portfolios of the SIEFOREs. Article 47 of the law authorizes CONSAR to permit each AFORE to
manage and offer to its clients a variety of SIEFOREs with different portfolio compositions, provided that
each AFORE offers at least one SIEFORE whose portfolio is comprised "fundamentally" of securities
whose returns are indexed to the Mexican CPI.
2.1    Phase I - Introduction of the investment regime
On the basis of the above legal provisions, CONSAR issued a detailed set of regulations governing the
investments of the SIEFOREs in June 1997. The regulations governing portfolios of the first SIEFORE to
be offered by the AFOREs are summarized in Table 1 below.
These guidelines governing SIEFORE investments are quite restrictive from an asset manager's
perspective. At the broadest level, since only domestic peso denominated fixed-income (debt) instruments
are allowed, the regulations do not permit the contributors to the SIEFOREs to benefit from diversification
across different asset classes such as domestic and international equities, international bonds, and private
equity. The strong restrictions on investment in non-government debt instruments essentially imply that
most SIEFORE investment will occur in government bonds. In addition, the low level of development of the
government bond markets have the effect that the investments of most SIEFOREs are likely to be invested
in identical government securities. Most of these instruments have very short maturities, which causes a
duration mismatch between assets and liabilities.

(1) Short-term obligations (less than one year maturity) must be rated in the top three rating categories by an authorized rating agency, long-term
obligations must be rated in the top two categories. CONSAR's Risk Analysis Committee will establish criteria for when a rating from a single agency
is inadequate and when commercial banks would be required to secure a rating from an additional agency.
(2) The securities of banks that have undergone intervention by the authorities are ineligible for investment by SIEFORES.
The rationale for the restrictive guidelines was the government's sensitivity to ensuring that the new system
got off to a sound slart and that any unexpected economic developments did not create a loss of credibility
for the system. The situation of the financial markets where the SIEFORE investments would be made was
(and to a large extent continues to be) less than fully satisfactory. One of the key sensitivities was to
disassociate the AFOREs from the mutual fund industry in Mexico, which has had a mixed reputation. The
restriction on investments in bank instruments was driven by the perceived fragility of the banking system,
especially as regards the capital adequacy of certain banks. The concentrated nature of the Mexican
financial sector also led to the strong restrictions on investments in related companies or entities. CONSAR
was also conscious of the need to simplify its own supervision during the transition to a new system, when
great demands were being made on all of its resources. Overall, in the early stages of operations of the new
system, ensuring the safety of the workers' savings and securing a reasonable rate of return was a greater
priority than pursuing an optimal asset allocation strategy.



Annex IV
Page 3 of 10
2.2    Phase II - Evolution of the investment regime
The experience of Mexico in actually operating under the investment regime outlined above highlighted
certain problems and constraints. As these have been identified, CONSAR has displayed considerable
ability and agility to respond to the needs of the evolving market. The main issues that have arisen
subsequent to implementation of the new system and CONSAR's attempts to fine tune the system by
addressing some of these issues through new regulations are discussed below. CONSAR has taken into
account the view points of the various market participants in formulating these regulations and their
modifications. A list of notifications issued and aspects dealt with by each notification is provided in Annex
III.
*   Investments in inflation-indexed bonds. A waiver has been granted temporarily to the
SIEFOREs on the earlier requirement that at least 51% of the SIEFORE investments be made
in inflation indexed securities (the requirement that at least 65% of all investments be in
government bonds continues). The reason for the waiver is that there were not enough
inflation-indexed instruments for the SIEFOREs to invest in. It is expected that the
government's own borrowing program from the market will provide these instruments over
time.
* Casht holdings. Earlier the SIEFOREs were not allowed to hold any cash balances. This
blanket ban on cash holdings created many operational problems for the SIEFOREs,
especially when there were any errors and omissions during the cash transfer process. In order
to simplify the process, the SIEFOREs are now allowed to hold up to P$250,000 in cash
balances.
*   Repurchase agreements. The requirement that SIEFOREs hold no cash also implied that all
the money received through contributions had to be invested immediately. Given the limited
number of securities that are allowed for investment and the fact all the cash came in on the
same day, the prices of the eligible securities were bid-up on the day that the cash was
received due to demand-supply imbalances. In order to allow SIEFOREs to better manage
their cash positions and time their investments, CONSAR has issued regulations allowing the
SIEFOREs to invest in repurchase agreements or repos. The amounts invested by a SIEFORE
in repos is restricted to the lower of: (i) the amount received in the most recent bimonthly
contributions; and (ii) 10% of the overall assets under management. For the next several
months, the second constraint will be binding. While the benefit of this regulation to the
SIEFOREs in liquidity management is significant. this regulation carries with it its own risks
and these are discussed in Section 3.4 below.
*  Asset valuation. CONSAR earlier issued guidelines that the valuation of the asset holdings of
the SIEFOREs should be done on a daily basis using a vector of prices published by the Bolsa
de Valores - the Mexican Stock Exchange. It was soon discovered that the prices received by
the SIEFOREs and CONSAR were sometimes erroneous (and amended later by the Bolsa on
the following day) or sometimes simply were not available. In this situation, fresh regulations
were issued to clarify that the SIEFOREs were authorized to use the most recent and accurate
price vector available to report valuations.
*   Performance reporting. Transparent reporting to the workers in the reformed system is critical
to ensure public support for the reforms and to establish credibility in the system. The law
goveming the new system commits the government to report both the returns on the



Annex IV
Page 4 of 10
investrnents by workers in the AFOREs and their contributions to INFONAVIT. In line with
this legislation, CONSAR issued regulations specifying the format in which the AFOREs will
report on the returns to the investors. The proposed reporting format has been provided in
Annex III.
* Others. CONSAR has clarified the earlier investment regulations so that the limits on various
investmnents should be adhered to at the time of investment. Changes in valuations due to
market movements do not constitute regulatory violations. This clarification is line with
international industry practice.
2.3    Current investments by AFOREs
Annex III provides details of the actual investments by AFOREs as of March 13, 98 based on the
investment regime discussed above. The AFOREs are largely investing in government bonds (CETES,
BONDES, and UDIBONOS) and the limit on private bonds (issued both by banks and corporates) is
currently not binding. Due to requirement that inflation-indexed bonds form a large part of the portfolio, the
AFOREs are investing proportionately more than the share of instruments available in the market in
UDIBONOS. Less than 0.25% of the aggregate investments are in bank securities and less than 1% of the
aggregate investments are in other private debt instruments. The average nominal annual rate of return
obtained by the AFOREs thus far is 21.5%, implying a real annualized rate of return of 6.2%.
3.    Compliance and risk management in the reformed system
In addition to providing the framework for the overall investment regime, CONSAR also has responsibility
to ensure compliarnce of the investment guidelines by the AFOREs. CONSAR also needs to ensure the
sustainability of the new system by getting a measure of, and then requiring the AFOREs to manage, the
risks to which the investors' funds are exposed. Once the fu.nds have been transferred to the AFOREs for
investment, the investors face several sources of risk. This section discusses various sources of risks and
methods to measure, monitor, and manage the risks.
At present, there is a significant overlap between the compliance and risk management functions of
CONSAR. As a regulator, it is essential that CONSAR perform the compliance function and provide
regulatory procedures to resolve violations with guidelines. As regards risk management, different
participants in the system have responsibilities to manage different risks. CONSAR should ideally focus on
system-wide risks and the AFOREs should be held accountable for portfolio and security level risks.
However, this is an area in which few market participants in Mexico have any extensive experience.
CONSAR's own experience is evolving and the Bank has played an active role in strengthening
CONSAR's capabilities by providing for system development and training through a Japanese PHRD
grant. At the present time, CONSAR is perforning most of the risk management functions (including those
that could potentially be performed by the AFOREs). Once the system is well established, CONSAR
should ideally take on a supervisory role and focus on monitoring the institutional risks of the system and
mandate minimum standards of performance from the AFOREs for the operational risks that each AFORE
faces. CONSAR should ensure compliance with guidelines by the AFOREs. These aspects are dealt with
in the recommendations set out below.
CONSAR has constituted two committees - the Comite de Analisis de Riesgos (the Risk Analysis
Conmmittee) and Comite de Valuacion (the Valuation Conunittee) at the time the new pensions law was
passed to oversee its operations in the risk management area. In order to achieve its compliance function,
CONSAR has put in place an extensive reporting system that obtains data from all the AFOREs. The
systems currently in place provide information to CONSAR about the specific investments being made by



Annex IV
Page S of 10
the AFOREs. CONSAR is also developing systems that would allow it to assess system-wide risks and
allow it to identify potential trouble spots.
3.1   Institutional risk
The primary risk is the institutional risk of the viability of the AFOREs themselves. The AFOREs have a
fiduciary responsibility to manage the funds in the best interests of the investors. The important issue of
safety of funds and protection from mismanagement by AFOREs has been addressed through various
regulations. The AFOREs have been established as single purpose companies capitalized separately from
any other members of any financial groups. The assets are held in the SIEFOREs - a separate legal entity -
whose assets are not available to meet the obligations of the sponsors of the AFOREs. CONSAR's role in
addressing institutional risks is part of its supervisory function. CONSAR has established an extensive
system of compliance monitoring in which AFOREs provide detailed reporting on their activities, financial
status, management etc. on a regular basis.
One issue of special concern at the institutional level is the potential consolidation of the industry. Strong
competition to attract affiliates has meant that all AFOREs incurred significant expenditures up-front on
advertising and sales forces. However, some AFOREs have not succeeded in attracting a sufficient number
of affiliates (see Annex III) and hence their financial viability is in question. This implies that a natural
consolidation may happen soon in the industry wherein smaller, less profitable AFOREs merge with others
AFOREs or are taken over by new groups interested in entering the business. CONSAR needs to ensure
that such a process is smoothly managed with the public adequately informed that their assets are safe
despite the consolidation. This would require both a public information campaign as well as strengthening
of CONSAR's own capacities to handle these issues.
3.2    Market Risk
CONSAR obtains information from all AFOREs on a daily basis on their positions in the individual
securities, the characteristics of the individual securities in terms of coupon, maturity, etc. and the
commissions paid by the AFOREs for these securities. Appropriate regulations to ensure such information
provision have been issued. CONSAR also collects extensive market information from on-line sources such
as Infosel, BBE and Bloomberg (on-line data providers). The data obtained through these sources cover
aspects such as government bond rates, details of new issues, ratings of private sector issuers etc.
Using the above information on the actual positions of the SIEFOREs and the market-wide information,
CONSAR is in the process of finalizing the use of two risk management software systems - RiskWatch
(developed by Algorithmics) and SIMAR ( Sistema para Manejar Riesgos- developed by JP Morgan) to
assess the risks implied by the positions of the SIEFOREs. The process of assessing the risk implied by the
positions of the various SIEFOREs involves assessing the sensitivity of the price of the securities (taking
into account correlations between various securities in the portfolio) to different market scenarios.
Weighting the sensitivity of the price of the instruments with the probability distribution of the occurrence
of the scenarios gives a measure of the value at risk. CONSAR would then be able to obtain risk reports on
each SIEFORE. CONSAR will also be able to obtain an idea of the aggregate risks across all AFOREs
since it has all the data.



Annex IV
Page 6 of 10
3.3    Credit risk
In addition to the above analysis that focuses on market risk, CONSAR is also engaged in monitoring the
credit risk of private sector instruments that have been allowed for investment by the investment
regulations. Currentlly, the guidelines allow the AFOREs to invest up to a total of 35% of their assets in
private debt instruments (such as certificates of deposit arnd corporate bonds) issued by banks and
corporates. Of this amount, AFOREs are allowed to invest no more than 10% of their assets in debt issued
by banks. All private instruments have to be rated a high investment grade(A or higher for short term and
AA or higher for medium/long term instruments) by one of the three approved rating agencies. At present
only four private banks (two domestic and two foreign) have outstanding short term debt with this high
rating. The number of corporates with highly rated debt instruments is also small. As of 13 March, 98 the
AFOREs had invested less that 1.25% of their aggregate portfolios in private debt instruments.
These private instruments carry with them credit risk which arises from the possibility that either the banks
or the corporates may default on their debt obligations. While, in theory, such default risk should be
factored into the price of the instrument, the lack of well developed capital markets implies that this may
not be the case. Hence, in order to ensure that the securities that the AFOREs invest in are adequately
secure, CONSAR monitors the credit risk of private instruments. CONSAR has done work on estimating
transition probabilities of private debt instruments moving from one credit rating to another. These
transition matrices are designed to assist CONSAR in assessing the credit risk of these instruments which
can then combined with the market risk factors to obtain an assessment of the overall risk of these
instruments. It must be noted that this is a function better performed by rating agencies as part of their
credit risk monitoring process and the AFOREs as part of their investment process. CONSAR should
therefore consider a gradual exit from such activities to more regulatory and supervisory activities.
3.4    Liquidity Risk
Managing liquidity risk is one of the major concerns of the SI]EFOREs in the current Mexican investment
environment and the structure of the cash flows in the reformed pension system. Cash comes in to the
AFOREs once every two months and essentially all of it (save some minor errors and omissions) comes in
on one day. Given the prevailing high real interest rates, the opportunity cost of holding cash is very high.
The investment regulations are quite restrictive and allow investment of the cash in a few instruments.
Finally, there simply are not enough instruments in some of the approved categories of investments for the
SIEFOREs to invest in. As a result SIEFOREs have already faced the problem of the market in the
approved instruments being tight (or cornered) by the brokers on the day in which the cash comes in.
Given these problems, CONSAR has recently issued new guidelines that allow SIEFOREs to enter into
repurchase agreements with banks using government securities as collateral. In this manner, the expectation
is that SIEFOREs can better manage their liquidity and have flexibility on their timing of entering into the
market while not having to sit on uninvested cash. In principle, this is a sound piece of regulation addressed
at managing liquidity risk. However, given the current state of the Mexican market it poses its own
dilemma.
In entering into a repo transaction, the SIEFOREs are essentially taking on bank credit risk, even though
the collateral is a government security. Given the fragile state of some of the Mexican banks, this issue is
critical. In any repo transaction, there is always an interest ralte risk. In the Mexican environment the credit
risk is likely to increase significantly at the sanie time as the interest rate risk rises. For e.g.: if a SIEFORE
enters into a repo agreement for 14 days (the maximum allowed now is 28 days) and in that time interest
rates rise significantly due to a systemic problem (interest rate swings of up to 200 basis points in a single
day are not uncommon), the SIEFORE ends up carrying high bank credit risk just as the incentives for the



Annex IV
Page 7 of 10
bank to return the cash it has obtained are significantly lower (the value of the collateral it has provided has
just fallen with the rise in interest rates). In the context of a fragile banking system, this issue needs to be
addressed. One option could be for the Central Bank to provide a repo window directly to the SIEFOREs
without going through the banks.
3.5    Recommendations
The main recommendations for future action by CONSAR in this area are:
*   Clearly separate the compliance function for the investment process from the risk management
function.
*   Institutionalize procedures to address systemic risks - especially procedures to resolve
AFORE mergers and bankruptcies.
*   Ensure that AFOREs have the capacity to take on the risk management function at SIEFORE
and security level.
*   Establish uniform standards of risk reporting by AFOREs.
*   Move to a supervisory role - ensuring compliance with guidelines and inspection of risk
reports from AFOREs.
* .Strengthen internal capacity to oversee risk management function and provide institutional
strengthening to AFOREs, if necessary.
*   Study the issue of the credit risk of repos and institute alternate mechanisms to provide a
liquidity window for the AFOREs.
4.     Systemic issues - interactions with the rest of the financial sector
The reformed pension system has extensive linkages with the rest of the Mexican financial sector (Annex
V) and these linkages introduce problems and issues that affect the entire system. These solutions to these
issues are more medium-long term in nature and involve coordinated action by CONSAR with other
financial sector regulators and participants in the financial sector. It is important to highlight these issues
and consider specific courses of action to address them in order to ensure that the investment of the pension
fund assets occurs in the best interest of the contributors.
4.1    Processing delays from point of payment to receipt by AFOREs
In the current system, there is a significant delay between the time at which the payments are made by the
employers, on behalf of the workers, to the collection agencies and the time at which the AFOREs actually
receive the money so that it can be invested. Currently, this time is about 9-10 business days. This implies
that for a significant amount of time, the payments of the workers are not invested and hence, are not
eaming any retums. While the cost of not being invested is high in almost any environment it is especially
so in an environment where the real interest rates on government instruments are about 5%1.
At a 5% real rate of interest, a worker loses approximately 4.8% of her real annual returns due to processing
delays of 10 days per contribution.



Annex IV
Page 8 of 10
It is critical that all the parties involved - CONSAR, IMSS, PROCESAR, the AFOREs, the banks through
the payments are made and Banco de Mexico all coordinate to reduce this time in which the cash is in
transit and hence idle to the minimum. This would improve the rates of return on the investments
significantly. The cost of not being invested is an opportunity cost in addition to any explicit transactions
costs that are charged by various participants.
4.2    Valiuation issues
All SIEFOREs are required to report the value of their asset holdings on a daily basis to CONSAR. On a
weekly basis, the value of the asset holdings is reported in the popular press by all SIEFOREs. All
SIEFOREs are required to mark their asset holdings to market daily i.e. the holdings are valued at the
prevailing market price (as opposed to other valuation bases such as cost price or book value) for the
respective instrument on the valuation day. A Valuation Committee has been set up with representatives of
CNBV, Hacienda, Banco de Mexico, CNSF, and CONSAR which establishes the guidelines for valuation
of various instruments. The brokerage houses, banks, mutual ifunds, and SIEFOREs are all required to use
the guidelines established by the Valuation Committee to imark-to-market their assets. The Valuation
Committee has established different guidelines for valuation of different securities - equities, fixed income
instruments, and repurchase agreements (repos). While the logic behind these guidelines is sound, there are
practical constraints in the application of these guidelines.
For fixed income instrument, while the concept of marking-to-market is well established, the main
complication that arises in the Mexican market is the lack of liquidity, and hence the absence of a clearly
market determined price, for several instruments. For e.g.: the greatest liquidity exists in instruments which
have maturities of 1, 7, 28, 90, etc. days. For any intermedliate date, the liquidity is extremely low and
hence market prices are essentially non-existent. In order to address this issue, the Valuation Committee
has authorized the use a price vector as a homogenous basis for pricing financial instruments by all market
participants.
The price vector is a polynomial function that is fitted to the market prices at the maturities that are traded
and available and extrapolated so that prices across all maturities are available (including those maturities
where there has been no trading and hence no market prices). Separate price functions are constructed for
different instruments such as CETES, BONDES, AJUSTABONOS etc. The price vector therefore uses as
inputs the traded market prices of securities at the most popular maturities and produces as outputs the
prices of securities with maturities at all intermediate dates. The Boisa Mexicana de Valores publishes
daily bulletins with the prices of various instruments for different maturities.
The SIEFOREs are required to use the daily outputs of the price vector in valuing their debt instrument
holdings. The advauntage of the price vector is that it provi,des a good measure of relative performance
across SIEFOREs (and other market participants with similar assets such as mutual funds) since all the
SIEFOREs use the same prices for valuing their holdings. The pricing function also solves the problem of
illiquid markets and lack of trades in many instruments. The disadvantage of the pricing function though is
that since the prices of instruments with "intermediate" maturities are not market determined and are the
outputs of a fitted model, prices of instruments with maturities very close to a traded instruments
sometimes are far from the market prices. The degree of "goodness of fit" of the model determines the
closeness of model prices to market prices. Hence the return volatility of the SIEFORE portfolios can be
significantly different from the market volatility.
Summarizing, the pricing function is a good basis for relative performance evaluation across SIEFOREs,
but is not fully satisfactory when the requirement is to cormpare performance relative to market. While
further refinements on certain technical aspects of the pricing vector may improve its performance, the



Annex IV
Page 9 of 10
underlying lack of liquidity in the market continues to be the core of the problem and needs to be addressed
in the long term. In the context of the SIEFOREs, this issue is tied into the issue of a market determined
benchmark for performance evaluation and this issue is addressed next.
4.3    Performance reporting and attribution - creating a benchmark
A critical issue related to several others such as risk management, performance reporting, and performance
attribution is that of having an appropriate performance benchmark. A benchmark is especially important
in the context of the Mexican system which does not provide any return guarantees to its participants.
Ensuring adequate performance is a prime responsibility of the government as the fiduciary of assets of the
workers. The lifetime switch option given to the workers in the old system further strengthens the case for
establishing a sound benchmark.
An objective, market determined benchmark is essential in assessing performance and in attributing
performance to specific manager decisions which add or detract value. A benchmark also provides the
ability to create a passive portfolio - one which assures the "market" return - similar to the widely known
stock market benchmarks in the US such as the S&P 500 index. At present, there is no market determined
benchmark for fixed income instruments in Mexico. In the present scenario, CONSAR calculates for
internal purposes, the performance of all the SIEFOREs and is able to rank them only in terms of relative
performance. However, this information has limited relevance in the absence of an absolute benchmark.
A benchmark is also integral for the purposes of assessing investment risk. Risk of a SIEFORE portfolio is
a measure of how different the returns of the SIEFORE are relative to the benchmark (measured by the
standard deviation of the excess returns of the portfolio relative to the benchmark). Since the benchmark,
by construction, removes market risk, any risk beyond the benchmark needs to measured and the excess
returns of SIEFORE portfolios should be assessed relative to this risk.
4.4    Establishing custodial services
Mexico currently has a central depository for its securities - the INDEVAL. However, it has no legal
provisions for a custodial system. The development of a custodial system would truly insulate the assets of
the workers from the AFOREs and provide significantly greater protection to the investors. While the legal
separation of the AFOREs from the SIEFOREs is a step in the right direction, the logical conclusion of this
process is the institutionalization of a custodial system in the country. Mexico already offers local custodial
services for its international investors, but does not have a provision for a similar system domestically. The
introduction of a custodial system would ensure safety of financial assets and has benefits for the capital
market development in general, much beyond its importance for the AFORE system. CONSAR could play
a useful role in assisting in the development of a custodial system by requiring the assets of AFOREs to be
custodied with independent custodians.
4.5    Portfolio diversification - one SIEFORE vs. multiple SIEFOREs
The pension law allows for the possibility that each AFORE may manage multiple SIEFOREs, while not
permitting more than one SIEFORE before July 1998. CONSAR and the private market participants have
discussed at length the issues in the expansion of workers' choice of SIEFOREs. The benefit of permitting
additional SIEFOREs - likely having a different asset allocation to that currently permitted and investing in
stocks and other securities - is to allow workers the maximum flexibility in tailoring their investrnents to
their desired risk-return profile. However, there are some important considerations that could place
constraints on the pace with which regulations are introduced to allow multiple SIEFOREs. The most
important problem is that of creating confusion among the workers with multiple SIEFOREs in a newly



Annex IV
Page 10 of 10
introduced system. It is not clear that adequate financial sophistication exists for correct choices to be
made. The government also needs to take into account the life-time switch option that it has given the
workers in the old system and the moral hazard associated with it for workers - especially those not
expecting a pension beyond the minimum guaranteed by the Government. Additional SIEFOREs will also
place additional demands on CONSAR, whose own capabilities are quite stretched at present. CONSAR is
presently studying the possibility of allowing multiple SIEFOREs and the financial as well as the
operational implications of such a decision.
4.6    Recommendations
*   Reduce the time taken between the payment of contributions by workers and receipt by
AFOREs. A study of the entire cash collection and transfer process, sources of delay and
methods to reduce the idle time of the cash should be conducted and recommendations
implemented.
*   Improve the secondary markets for trading of government debt which will improve the
enviroinment for investment by AFOREs. Such an improvement is a long term issue, but one
which the government should focus on to improve the entire financial system, part of which
are the AFOREs. The valuation issue will be a much smaller problem once secondary markets
develop.
   Establish a market determined benchmark for performance evaluation and risk assessment of
AFORE performance. This would involve CONSAR interacting with private market
participants to develop and maintain a benchmark on an ongoing basis. Existing measures of
returns on Mexican fixed-income markets (such as J.P. Morgan's ELBI index) should be
examined for applicability.
*   Address development of the corporate bond market, strengthen rating agencies. These are long
term issues and the presence of the AFOREs could provide a Jemand driven solution to this
problem. This issue is related to many other financial market development issues such as
corporate governance, bankruptcy legislation, legal rights of bondholders, etc.
*   Implement a custodial system for AFORE and mutual fund assets. The custody issue has
important legal dimensions that need to be addressed. CONSAR would finalize a study on the
issue. On the basis of the study, necessary legislation may need to be drafted and/or CONSAR
shoulcd issue guidelines ensuring that SIEFORE assets are custodied safely.
*   Conduct a study on the introduction of multiple SIEFOREs and take a decision based on the
recommendations.
5.     Conclusion
As an overall assessment, the process of introduction of the regulations to govem investments of the
AFOREs has got off to a good start. While CONSAR agrees that the present investment regime is too
restrictive, it is in line with the regulatory structure of most new privately managed pension systems. As
expected, there are many issues that need to be addressed in order to put the system on a more sustainable
basis and to address the various risks in the system. It is important to appreciate that the system is very
new - it is less than a year old in terms of full operations. It is hence an evolving system.



Annex V
Page I of 8
MEMCO
SECOND CONTRACTUAL SAVINGS DEVELOPMENT PROGRAM
COM:PLEMENTARY FINANCL4L SECTOR REFORMS
The sustainability of the Mexican pension reform hinges on implementation of complementary financial
sector reform. A funded, privatized pension system needs adequate securities to invest in and appropriate
institutional structures to ensure the safety of those securities. The pension system is an integral part of the
overall financial sector and hence the performance of the pension system is linked to the health of the
overall financial sector. Success of the pension reform is therefore linked to reforms in the rest of the
financial sector. The Government recognizes this link and is undertaking a series of steps aimed at
strengthening various institutions in the financial sector.
1. The Banking Sector
Mexico's financial system continues to be dominated by commercial banks and their financial holding
companies. These banks were hard hit by the devaluation of the peso in late 1994. The ensuing crisis in
1995 revealed and deepened the already poor asset quality in banks' balance sheets. The Government has
taken steps to restructure the banking system and increase public confidence in the banking system, though
at high fiscal costs (estimated at over 14% of 1997 GDP). The sector has been opened up to foreign
investments, supervision and regulation have been improved and universal deposit insurance
(FOBAPROA) is now available. In addition, several systemic issues are being addressed including the
fiscal recognition of the costs of the banking crisis.
Table 1 shows the evolution of the Mexican financial system since the crisis in 1994. It is clear that the
domestic banking sector has suffered a sharp contraction, while foreign banks have increased their presence
in the market. The non-bank financial institution sector including leasing and factoring intermediaries and
foreign exchange trading houses has also contracted. Mutual funds have declined dramatically both in
numbers and in size of operations (see section 2).
In designing the new pension system, the Government took various steps to insulate the AFOREs from the
fragile banking system:
* The influence of the banks on the management of the AFOREs was minimized through the
requirement that all AFOREs be established as single-purpose separately capitalized companies. The
capital of an AFORE that is a subsidiary of a financial group is not available to meet the obligations of
other subsidiaries of the group.
* As an additional safeguard Article 22 of the Pension Systems Law provides that financial
intermediaries (including banks) or financial groups that are not in full compliance with applicable
capital standards may not be shareholders of an AFORE'.
* Under the Pension Systems Law, a shareholder that at any time wishes to acquire more than 10% of the
capital stock of an AFORE is required to make detailed financial disclosures to the CONSAR revealing
their sources of capital for the preceding five years.
Mexican Capital Adequacy Requirement (CAR) rules differ from those of the Bank for International Settlements
(BIS) in important ways, including the treatment of subordinated and mandatorily convertible debt into equity and
the revaluation of assets.



Annex V
Page 2 of 8
*  The extent of pension fund investment in the bankinrg sector was also limited by requiring that
SIEFOREs be permitted to hold a maximum of 10% of their assets in bank securities.
*  A further regulation requiring that this 10% investment be made only in bank instruments with a high
credit rating (AA or higher (or equivalent) for long-term debt and A or higher (or equivalent) for short-
term debt by any of the three approved rating agencies) ensures that the pension assets will be invested
only in high quality instruments. As pension funds increase in size and become significant institutional
investors, it can be expected that the competition amongt banks for AFORE investments will increase.
As the banking sector becomes stronger, more avenues for investment by AFOREs will open up.

As of March 13, 1]998, the AFOREs had invested an extremely small portion of their aggregate portfolios
(less than 0.25%) in bank instruments. This exposure is due: to the investment of about 3.8% of the assets
of one AFORE (Santander Mexicano) in bank instruments. The instruments invested in are high quality
short term commercial papers. Hence, the limit of 10% of assets of each AFORE's assets is not binding at
the moment. The main reason for the low level of investments, in addition to tight regulation, is that on a
risk-adjusted basis; Govemment bonds are currently the highest yielding instruments in Mexico.
2. Mutual funds
The Mexican mutual fund industry began operations in an environment of extremely high effective reserve
requirements (over 90%) imposed by the central bank in the early eighties. Since almost no cash was
available for regular bank lending operations funded by deposits, mutual funds were used as a source of
funding to circumvent the high reserve requirements. Hence, mutual funds in Mexico have traditionally
invested in illiquidl loan assets which, under normal circumstances, would have been funded with deposits.
In addition, the supervision of these funds was poor and disclosure and investor protection norms were lax.
The privatization of the banking sector in 1992 accompanied by the sharp drop in real interest rates ensured
that the market vaLlue of the funds appreciated sharply and hence the size of the industry increased. In the
wake of the 1994 peso crisis and sharply higher real interest rates, the value of these funds dramatically
declined and individual investors were hard hit. Lower resenre requirements meant that the banks could



Annex V
Page 3 of 8
fund loan operations from deposits and hence the importance of mutual funds as funding sources declined.
The industry has contracted sharply since 1994. In terms of real value of assets under management, it is
currently one-third of its size in 1994.
Mutual funds in Mexico are currently governed by the Ley de Sociedades de Inversion enacted in 1987.
There are four types of mutual funds in operation in Mexico - equity funds, debt funds for institutional
investors, debt funds for individual investors, and venture capital or private equity funds. The assets are
managed largely by banks and subsidiaries of financial groups. Supervision of mutual funds and mutual
fund operating companies are entrusted to CNBV, and all relevant information must be submitted to its
review. CNBV's supervisory responsibilities include registration of mutual funds, establishing disclosure
guidelines and on-site and off-site monitoring of compliance. If, as a result of inspection, a mutual fund or
an operating company is found not to be in compliance with CNBV's rules, CNBV has the right to
intervene or sanction the institution.
As of January 31, 1998 the Mexican mutual fund industry consisted of 345 mutual funds and 39 mutual
fund operating companies, with total assets under management of $13.8 billion with an investor base of
about 321,000 investors. Almost $11 billion of assets are invested in debt instruments and the balance in
equities. The industry has been characterized by volatility in both the number of investors and assets under
management. The trend has been that individual small investors have been declining and the funds have
become more institutional. The size of the industry, measured by the aggregate amount of assets under
management as a percentage of GDP, has been in the range of 3.2-3.4% since 1995. Mutual fund
shareholders in Mexican population is currently 0.3%, a fifth of the population with bank accounts. This
percentage has been gradually declining since the early 1990's. Performance data on an industry wide-basis
along with an appropriate comparative benchmark is unavailable.
The Ley de los Sistemas de Ahorro para el Retiro pointedly did not adopt the governance and supervisory
framework applicable to Mexico's mutual fund industry. The Government felt that since a handful of
financial groups control the bulk of the Mexican banking, securities and mutual funds industries, the
legal/regulatory framework for a mandatory contribution system should impose stricter rules for
independent decision-making and avoidance of conflicts of interest.
The introduction of the private pension system has had a salutary effect on the regulation of the mutual
fund sector in Mexico. Recognizing the weaknesses in the regulatory structure for mutual funds, the CNBV
is currently considering reforms which would bring the mutual fund regulation more in line with the
pension fund regulation. As part of the reforms, the CNBV is considering the introduction of a mutual fund
rating system along the lines of those published by Morningstar in the US. These ratings are proposed to be
done by the rating agencies (a discussion of the rating industry in Mexico is provided below). There is also
an active ongoing debate in Mexico among the regulators and the mutual fund industry about other reforms
that may be needed to encourage mutual funds as a viable savings vehicle. Deregulation of fee structures,
increased portfolio disclosure, standardized reporting norms, and stricter fiduciary standards such as
imposition of prudent man/prudent investor rules are some of the issues under discussion.

3. Voluntary pension plans
Several Mexican corporations have established voluntary pension plans for their workers over and above
the mandatory social security programs. These schemes involve employee contributions of between 2-6%
with the plan assets managed by a bank or a brokerage firm. These plans are essentially unregulated,
although they are subject certain broad portfolio requirement by the SHCP. These plans have been
established mostly by large private sector firms as well as Mexican subsidiaries of multinational firms.
As the supervisor of the new mandatory pension system, CONSAR is exploring the possibility of bringing
the voluntary pension plans under its supervision. CONSAR plans to issue regulations by which a
voluntary pension lplan can be registered with CONSAR. Such registration will, at least initially, be
voluntary. Pension plans choosing to be registered need to be certified as actuarially sound and maintain
that status on an ongoing basis. Participants of plans that register with CONSAR and subject themselves to
its supervision have: significant advantages such as the ability to transfer their retirement accounts' at the
AFOREs to their private plan upon retirement and receive pensions under the terms of the voluntary plan.
As many voluntary plans provide pensions at an earlier age than the mandatory system, such flexibility is
attractive to the workers. The AFOREs are also permitted to establish a separate sub-account for the
workers for voluntary contributions in addition to the mandatory portion. Table 3 shows the voluntary
contributions by AFORE received till March 1998.



Annex V
Page S of 8
The response to CONSAR's initial efforts at registering voluntary pension plans has not been enthusiastic.
Out of the estimated 1000 private pension funds in operation only three have registered with CONSAR as
of March 13, 1998. The most plausible reason for the lack of interest is that many are actuarially
underfunded and the firms would require to first fund the plans before being eligible for registration. The
Government needs to take a serious view of this situation and take appropriate action to ensure safety of the
workers funds in voluntary pension plans. As a first step it would be useful to conduct a study to get an
assessment of the size of the voluntary pension plans, their actuarial funded status, and investment patterns.
Suitable policy actions can be taken on the basis of such a study. CONSAR has also undertaken a drive to
register all the practicing actuaries in Mexico under the assumption that greater knowledge of the state of
the voluntary pension fund industry can be obtained by having a formal relationship with the actuaries.
Thus far, 26 of the 40 qualified actuaries have voluntarily registered themselves with CONSAR. The type
of information that they will provide CONSAR is being discussed.
4. Development of Long-Term Instruments and Financial Services
Long-term government debt markets. With the reform of the social security system, there is a pool of
funds available for the first time that needs to be invested in order to provide a stream of pension payments
to the workers upon their retirement. Pension liabilities are typically long term and the natural asset to
hedge such liabilities is a long duration instrument such as long term bonds or equities. Mexico's
Govermnent bond markets are poorly developed and provide mostly short-term (less than one year
duration) bonds. The composition of trading in the secondary markets also provides an indication of the
short-term nature of the market. Over 95% of all trades - both in the Bolsa and over-the-counter - are in
overnight securities. There is almost no liquidity in long-term instruments. While historically the cause of
this underdeveloped state may have been the chronic inflation, it is essential that now the Government
considers development of this market segment.
Corporate debt market. The heavy presence of the banking sector in Mexico's economy has traditionally
implied that the level of intermediation has been very high. Most corporate lending was from banks, that in
turn financed these assets from deposits. The presence of institutional pension funds provides a natural
alternate source of funds for high quality corporates to raise funds. Development of a sound corporate debt
market would provide another investment vehicle for the pension funds. The investment regulations permit
investment up to 35% in rated private debt instruments. However, the actual investment by the SIEFOREs
in private instruments is very small (less than 1% up to March 13, 1998). There are several reasons for the
low level of investments in private sector instruments.
One of the major problems is the lack of adequate highly-rated corporate paper that qualifies for AFORE
investment. Table 4 provides details of the corporate debt market. CNBV's internal analysis shows the
extent of problems in timely payment of principal and interest for many of the corporate debt issues. In
general, the instruments range in maturity from 5-10 years, with an average maturity of 7 years.
The secondary market for most issues is very illiquid (or non-existent). A very small number of issues are
rated investment grade (out of a small universe of rated securities) and hence the market in high quality
private debt instruments is small, relative to the market for Government paper. A second - though smaller -
problem is the differential fiscal treatment given to private debt issuers vs. Government debt issues. The
withholding tax of 2% on private debt issues often makes the after-tax rate of return on private, more risky
issues less than Government paper. Yet another problem is that the debt market does not provide an
adequate compensation for risk in terms of differing level of interest rates for different credit risks. There
are currently several low rated corporate securities in the market that yield a lower return that higher rated
securities. The market is driven more by the name of the issuer rather than the structure of the specific



Annex V
Page 6 of 8
issue. This lack of appropriate risk-return relationship further discourages investments in debt issues. There
are also some doubts in the market on the objectivity of the raLtings.

Housing mortgage market. Long term securitized housing mortgages are a good investment vehicle for
pension funds which have long-term liabilities. The availabil:ity of long term investors should also help to
stimulate the housing market. However, the quality of the investments depends to a large extent on the
availability of appropriate instruments and efficiency of the mortgage market. The Governm. ent is taking an
active role in restructuring the existing housing finance market. It is in the process of making the operations
of FOVI more marlcet-oriented and the operations of INFONAVIT more transparent. The Bank is working
closely with the Government on both these institutions. One of the major hurdles to securitization in
Mexico is the presence of instruments whose returns are not market determined. For example, the interest
rates on some mortgage instruments are a function of the minimum wage in the economy, which is a
Government detemiined policy variable. Development of an efficient housing mortgage market requires the
introduction of new market-based mortgage instruments, transparent and consistent origination standards,
presence of a mortgage insurer, and a strong rating industry whose ratings are viewed as objective and
reliable.

While these problems are not unique to Mexico and are widespread across many developing financial
markets, there is a need to address these issues in Mexico as part of the financial and capital market
reforms complementary to the pension reforms. A sound debt market is critical to long term capital
mobilization and assessment of corporate risk and hence sound allocation of capital.
Financial intermediaries. Credit rating agencies, custodial services, third-party performance assessment
services (similar to Morningstar and Lipper in the US) are some of the other players in the overall financial
infrastructure that are required for maximizing the economic benefits of a privatized pension system.
Mexico currently has three rating agencies, a central depository that offers partial custodial services, and
no independent perfornance assessment agencies. The Government should examine these segments of the
financial infrastructure and take appropriate policy actions to assist in their development as part of the
overall development of the financial sector.
Rating agencies. There are currently three credit rating agencies in Mexico - all subsidiaries of US-based
credit rating agencies. These are (i) Standard & Poor's, (ii) Duff & Phelps, and (iii) Fitch2. The rating
methodology and systems have been adapted for the Mexican market from those in the US. the rating
industry began in 1989 and has developed slowly, given the volatility in the debt markets in Mexico. At
present, the rating agencies rate mainly short term commnercial paper issued by corporates and banks. Other
2 Fitch is in the process of acquiring 100% control of a domestic credit rating agency - Fitch-Clase - in which it
currently has a minority shareholding. This acquisition is expected to be completed by June 1998.



Annex V
Page 8 of 8
market segments covered include corporate debt issues, structured instruments such as mortgage backed
securities, securitizations of assets such as credit card receivables, and debt issues of insurance companies.
As of March 1998, albout 180 debt issues (out of a market of about 475 issues) had been rated in Mexico.
Since January 1, 1998 CNBV requires all debt mutual funds to be rated and this has provided another
source of business for the rating agencies. They are rating both the portfolios of the mutual funds and the
mutual fund companies3. Multiple ratings of the same issue is becoming more popular in Mexico and
should help investors obtain a better perspective of the risks of the instrument. However, the practice of
"rating shopping" - an issuer looking for the best ratings at the lowest price - is prevalent and hence colors
the perceptions of the market on the objectivity of the ratings. CNBV is also considering further regulation
of the rating agencies, although the nature and extent of the regulation is still unclear. The Government
should be aware that substituting ratings in place of regulations requiring greater corporate transparency
and disclosure is likely to politicize ratings and hence reduce their effectiveness as a tool for greater market
efficiency. Rating agencies should be allowed to comment freely on credit risks and provide a credible and
objective source of risk analysis. The investment guidelines for the new pension system require the
AFOREs to invest only in rated private debt instruments (subject to a cap of 25% of assets). The
performance of the pension system therefore depends on the sound development and credibility of the rating
agencies.
Custodial services. A system of custody provides safety for the financial assets including cash of
investors and legally and physically separates the entity making the investment decision from the control
over actual financial assets. In a private pension system such as that in Mexico, a custodial arrangement
would ensure that the AFOREs only make the decision on where to invest the worker's contributions but
would otherwise have no legal control over either the cash contributions or the financial instruments at any
time. Mexico currently does not have a domestic custody industry4. INDEVAL - the central depository -
provides certain services similar to a custodian. However, the financial intermediaries still retain legal
control of both the cash contributions and financial assets. Regulations governing the AFOREs separate
their assets from all other members of the same financial group. While these regulations are sound, a
formal custody arrangement would enhance the safety of the assets. Development of custodial services
would assist many other segments of the financial sector such as mutual funds, insurance companies, and
voluntary pension funds.
3 It is important to note that in the US none of the three agencies are involved in rating mutual funds - whether the
funds invest in debt or other asset classes. Hence, it is not clear that the rating agencies have either a comparative
advantage or the technical expertise in this particular line of operations.
4 International investors in Mexican securities can obtain on-shore custodial services through branches of some
large multinational banks.

ANNEXXI
Page I of 2
MEXICO
SECOND CONTRACTUAL SAVINGS DEVELOPMENT PROGRAM
SUPPLEMENTARYDATA SHEET
Section I:   Timetable of Key Events
(a)   Time taken by the country
to prepare the loan:           9 months
(b)   Project prepared by:           Ministry of Finance (SHCP), CONSAR,
and INFONAVIT
(c)   First presentation to the Bank:  September 1997
(d)   Departure of Appraisal Mission:    May, 1998
(e)   Completion of Negotiations:    May, 1998
(f)   Planned date of effectiveness:  October, 1998
Section II:  Special Implementation Actions
None
Section III:  Special Conditions
Actions taken before Board Presentation:
1) Regulatory framework: CONSAR issued key regulations in the areas of: (a) disclosure; and
(b) administration of individual accounts still in the Central Bank, including (i) regulations
on the outsourcing of information to workers and (ii) related fees;
2) Operation and efficiency of new pension system: (a) CONSAR has authorized AFOREs in
a timely fashion; (b) CONSAR has issued general rules governing affiliation contract; (c)
affiliation of at least 60% of potential IMSS's workers; (d) CONSAR has issued regulations
relating to the switching among AFOREs; (e) CONSAR has issued regulations relating to the
functioning of the centralized collection and record-keeping system; (f) generation of CURPs
for at least 25% of potential affiliates; (g) the computerized reporting system (SUA) was
developed and implemented; (h) CONSAR has issued regulations on the maximum fees to be
charged by PROCESAR for processing, reconciling, and disbursing information on the
administration of individual accounts; and (i) CONSAR has finalized a study comparing
commissions charged by AFOREs in a standardized format;
3) Transfer of SAR92 balances: (a) CONSAR, SHCP, and Banco de Mexico (Central Bank)
have issued detailed guidelines to make operational transfer of SAR92 balances; and (b)
transfer of at least 20% of accumulated assets in SAR92 accounts at the Central Bank have
been transferred to AFOREs;
4) Investment policies and risk management: (a) CONSAR has issued necessary
modifications and changes to initial investment guidelines relating to investments in



ANAEXX[
Page 2 of 2
inflation-indexed bonds, cash holdings, repurchase agreements, and asset valuation; and (b)
CONSAR has established two committees to oversee its operations in the risk management
area;
5) Supervision and enforcement: CONSAR has issued regulations establishing the
methodology for the valuation of eligible instruments;
6) Disability and life (IV): (a) the Insurance Law was modified to incorporate the changes to
the provision of IV benefits under the reformed Social Security Law and to make operational
the provision of such benefits by authorized private insurance companies; (b) CNSF issued a
number of regulations to make operational the new scheme; and (c) a Department in charge
of supervision of pension-related insurance (Direcci6n General de Supervisi6n del Seguro de
Pensiones) was established within CNSF;
7) Update of fiscal costs of transition: the Government has completed the 1997 update to the
sensitivity analysis of the fiscal costs of transition and the evaluation of actuarial
methodologies and the data base;
8) INFONAVIT: (a) INFONAVIT, SHCP and CNBV signed an agreement on March 27, 1998,
and approved by INFONAVIT's Board of Governors on March 30, 1998, to restore
INFONAVIT to financial health within a five year period through a major operational and
financial restructuring program; (b) INFONAVIT has accepted new accounting standards to
be issued by CNBV which are consistent with the rules applicable to commercial banks for
mortgage loans; (c) INFONAVIT has stopped accruing, and created provisions against,
interest and indexation of non performing loans; and (d) INFONAVIT has provisioned for its
non performing loans as required by CNBV and based on the findings of the external
auditing firm and CNBV's inspections;
9) Preparatory work for the Public and State Pension systems (a) finalization of actuarial
and financial valuation of ISSSTE and initiation of detailed evaluation of feasibility and
fiscal costs of alternative reform strategies; and (b) finalization of initial survey of data bases
and actuarial and financial situation of 32 pension systems at the State level and approval of
standardized valuation format.
The following policy actions on INFONAVIT would be taken prior to effectiveness:
(a) establishment of an appropriate system of external financial controls beginning in 1999;
(b) submission of a revised table of maximum credit amounts agreed with CNBV and approved
by INFONAVIT's Board of Governors; and
(c) review of credit rules and loan rationing mechanism, and if necessary, submission of
revised rules to INFONAVIT's Board of Governors.
